Mutual Injuries with Different Weapons: Free Man vs Slave
Rulings on mutual injuries with sword vs stick

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for mutual injuries when weapons differ (sword vs stick) and identify the role of the 'a'qila and qisas.

Core Idea
Weapon type determines intent: a sword triggers qisas, a stick triggers 'a'qila liability.

This lesson examines scenarios where a free man and a slave fight with different weapons—one has a sword, the other a stick. The distinction is critical because the Hanafi school classifies killing with a stick as quasi-intentional (khata' al-'amd), shifting the diyah to the 'a'qila. We will follow the logical flow of the ruling and understand how the owner's statement resolves evidentiary gaps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and weapons
In each scenario, note who has the sword and who has the stick. This classification determines the nature of the injury (intentional vs quasi-intentional).
2
Determine the outcome (death or survival)
If the slave dies, the liability shifts; if both survive, the calculation of compensation (arsh) differs.
3
Apply the evidentiary principle (owner's statement)
When the initiator is unknown, the slave owner's statement is taken as authoritative regarding who started the fight.
This is based on the principle that the owner has superior knowledge of his slave's affairs.
4
Classify the killing according to weapon
A sword indicates 'amd (intentional killing) → qisas. A stick indicates khata' al-'amd → diyah on the 'a'qila.
'a'qila are the male agnate relatives responsible for paying diyah in quasi-intentional cases.
5
Calculate the financial compensation
Deduct the free man's injury (arsh) from the slave's value; the remainder goes to the owner.
If the free man is entitled to qisas, the owner may execute qisas, voiding the free man's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حر وعبد التقيا ومع كل واحد منهما عصا واضطربا فشج كل واحد صاحبه ثم اختلف مولى العبد والحر في البداءة فالقول للمولى أن الحر بدأ وعليه أرش جنايته على العبد للمولى، ثم يدفع العبد بجنايته أو يفديه لأن الحر أقر بأرش يد بالجناية لأنه ادعى الإبراء متى اختار المولى دفع العبد إليه وأنكر المولى فيكون القول له ولو كان مع العبد سيف ومع الحر عصا فمات العبد وبرأ الحر واختلفا كان القول للمولى وقيمة العبد على عاقلة الحر يسلم المولى من مقدار ما نقصه الحر من قيمته إلى يوم ضرب العبد الحر والباقي قيمة أرش جنايته على الحر، فإن فضل شيء فهو للمولى لأن الحر قتل بعصا فيكون قتيل خطأ العمد فيجب قيمته على عاقلة الحر والقيمة قامت مقام العبد كأن العبد حي فيأخذ المولى قدر ما انتقص بجناية الحر ويأخذ الحر من الباقي أرش جراحته، فإن فضل شيء منه فهو للمولى لأنه بدل عبده وقد فرغ العبد عن حق الغير، وإن انتقص الباقي لا يكون على المولى شيء كما لو دفع العبد وقيمته أقل أرش الجراحة. ولو كان السيف مع الحر ومع العبد عصا فمات العبد وبرأ الحر ولا يدري أيهما بدأ بالجناية فللمولى أن يقتل الحر ويبطل حق الحر لأن الحر قتل بالسيف عمداً فوجب القود فقد مات العبد ولم يخلف بدلاً فيبطل حق الحر، وكذلك لو كان العبد هو الذي بدأ بالجناية لأنه لا يتصور تملك العبد بسبب بعد ما مات ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما عصا فشج كل واحد منهما صاحبه موضحة وبرئا واتفقوا أنهم لا يعلمون البادئ من هو خير المولى، فإن دفع العبد يرجع على الحر بنصف أرش عبده لأن الحر إن كان البادئ بالجناية يجب عليه جميع أرش عبده، وإن كان اللاحق فهو لا يجب عليه شيء فيجب نصفه، وإن شاء فداه بجميع أرش الحر ورجع على الحر بجميع أرش عبده لأنه يجب على الحر جميع أرش العبد تقدمت جنايته أو تأخرت فإن كانا سواء اتفقا، وإن كان أحدهما أقل فالأقل بمثله قصاصاً يصير من ويرد الفضل على صاحبه.
A free man and a slave met, each with a stick, and they fought each other, causing a scalp wound to each other. Then the slave's owner and the free man differed about who started. The statement is that of the owner: the free man started, and he owes the arsh of his injury to the slave to the owner, then he hands over the slave for his injury or ransoms him, because the free man acknowledged the arsh of the hand due to the injury, since he claimed immunity when the owner chose to hand over the slave to him, and the owner denied, so the owner's statement is taken. If the slave had a sword and the free man had a stick, then the slave died and the free man recovered, and they differed, the owner's statement is taken. The value of the slave is upon the free man's 'a'qila. The owner subtracts the amount by which the free man decreased the slave's value up to the day the slave struck the free man, and the rest is the value of the arsh of his injury upon the free man. If anything remains, it is for the owner, because the free man killed with a stick, so it is a killing resembling error (khata' al-'amd), so its value is due on the free man's 'a'qila. The value stands in place of the slave as if the slave were alive, so the owner takes the amount diminished by the free man's injury, and the free man takes from the remainder the arsh of his wound. If anything remains, it is for the owner, as it is the substitute for his slave, and the slave is free from the claim of others. If the remainder is insufficient, nothing is owed by the owner, just as if he handed over the slave and his value is less than the arsh of the wound. If the sword was with the free man and the slave had a stick, then the slave died and the free man recovered, and they do not know which of them started the injury, then the owner may kill the free man and the free man's right is void, because the free man killed with a sword intentionally, so retaliation (qisas) is due. The slave has died and left no substitute, so the free man's right becomes void. Similarly, if the slave was the one who started the injury, because it is unimaginable that the slave would acquire ownership after his death. If each had a stick and they gave each other a scalp wound (mudihah) and recovered, and they agree that they do not know who started, the owner has the option: if he hands over the slave, he may claim from the free man half the arsh of his slave, because if the free man was the initiator, he owes the full arsh of the slave; if he was the subsequent, he owes nothing, so half is due. If he prefers, he may ransom the slave by paying the full arsh of the free man, and then claim from the free man the full arsh of his slave, because the free man owes the full arsh of the slave whether his injury preceded or followed. If they are equal (the wounds are of the same type), they offset each other; if one is less, the lesser is offset by its equivalent in qisas, and the excess is returned to its owner.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents several scenarios of mutual injuries between a free man and a slave, focusing on the determination of liability based on who started and the type of weapon used. The author rules that when both have sticks, the owner's statement is taken that the free man started, and the free man owes arsh for his own hand (in the initial scenario) and must either hand over the slave or ransom him. When the slave has a sword and the free man a stick, and the slave dies, the free man's 'a'qila pays the slave's value; the owner deducts the depreciation caused by the free man's injury, and the free man gets his arsh from the remainder. When the free man has a sword and the slave a stick, and the slave dies, the owner can execute qisas against the free man, voiding the free man's right because the slave is dead. Finally, when both have sticks and both receive mudihah, and the initiator is unknown, the owner may choose to hand over the slave and get half the slave's arsh from the free man, or ransom the slave by paying the free man's full arsh and then claim full arsh for his slave from the free man. The rulings are based on the principle that the law takes the statement of the one who has authority (the owner) and that the type of weapon classifies the homicide as 'amd (sword) or khata' al-'amd (stick).

Key Terms
خطأ العمد (khata' al-'amd): A category between intentional and accidental killing: the actor intended the act but did not intend to kill, e.g., striking with a stick.
عاقلة (al-'a'qila): The group of male agnate relatives (usually the clan) who are liable to pay diyah for accidental and quasi-intentional killings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where the slave has a sword and the free man has a stick, and the slave dies, who pays the slave's value?
A) The free man directly
B) The free man's 'a'qila
C) The owner himself
D) No one, because the slave attacked first

Key Takeaway
Weapon type determines the classification of homicide and thus the liability: sword → qisas, stick → diyah on 'a'qila.

Both with Sticks, Unknown Initiator: Owner's Options
Owner's options when both have sticks and initiator unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the slave owner when both parties have sticks and the initiator is unknown.

Core Idea
When the initiator is unknown, the owner may either hand over the slave and claim half arsh, or ransom the slave and claim full arsh.

This lesson focuses on the specific scenario where a free man and a slave both have sticks and give each other scalp wounds (mudihah), recover, and neither knows who started. The Hanafi school gives the slave owner two choices to ensure fair compensation for his slave's injury. We will explore the reasoning behind each option and the calculation of the amounts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the injury type and recovery
Both receive mudihah (a scalp wound exposing bone) and recover fully, so no death is involved.
Mudihah is a specific type of wound with a fixed arsh (1/20 of diyah).
2
Recognize the uncertainty about the initiator
Both parties agree that they do not know who started the fight.
In the absence of knowledge, the law relies on the owner's statement as established earlier.
3
Understand the owner's first option: hand over the slave
The owner may hand over the slave to the free man for his injury, and then claim from the free man half the arsh of his slave.
The free man owes the full arsh of the slave if he started, but nothing if he followed. Since it's unknown, half is due.
4
Understand the owner's second option: ransom the slave
The owner may pay the free man's full arsh (to ransom the slave), and then claim from the free man the full arsh of the slave.
This option assumes the free man started; the owner pays first and then collects the full arsh.
5
Apply the offset if wounds are equal
If the arsh amounts are equal, they offset. If one is less, the lesser is offset by qisas, and the excess is returned to its owner.
Qisas here means the wound is reciprocated; any imbalance is compensated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حر وعبد التقيا ومع كل واحد منهما عصا واضطربا فشج كل واحد صاحبه ثم اختلف مولى العبد والحر في البداءة فالقول للمولى أن الحر بدأ وعليه أرش جنايته على العبد للمولى، ثم يدفع العبد بجنايته أو يفديه لأن الحر أقر بأرش يد بالجناية لأنه ادعى الإبراء متى اختار المولى دفع العبد إليه وأنكر المولى فيكون القول له ولو كان مع العبد سيف ومع الحر عصا فمات العبد وبرأ الحر واختلفا كان القول للمولى وقيمة العبد على عاقلة الحر يسلم المولى من مقدار ما نقصه الحر من قيمته إلى يوم ضرب العبد الحر والباقي قيمة أرش جنايته على الحر، فإن فضل شيء فهو للمولى لأن الحر قتل بعصا فيكون قتيل خطأ العمد فيجب قيمته على عاقلة الحر والقيمة قامت مقام العبد كأن العبد حي فيأخذ المولى قدر ما انتقص بجناية الحر ويأخذ الحر من الباقي أرش جراحته، فإن فضل شيء منه فهو للمولى لأنه بدل عبده وقد فرغ العبد عن حق الغير، وإن انتقص الباقي لا يكون على المولى شيء كما لو دفع العبد وقيمته أقل أرش الجراحة. ولو كان السيف مع الحر ومع العبد عصا فمات العبد وبرأ الحر ولا يدري أيهما بدأ بالجناية فللمولى أن يقتل الحر ويبطل حق الحر لأن الحر قتل بالسيف عمداً فوجب القود فقد مات العبد ولم يخلف بدلاً فيبطل حق الحر، وكذلك لو كان العبد هو الذي بدأ بالجناية لأنه لا يتصور تملك العبد بسبب بعد ما مات ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما عصا فشج كل واحد منهما صاحبه موضحة وبرئا واتفقوا أنهم لا يعلمون البادئ من هو خير المولى، فإن دفع العبد يرجع على الحر بنصف أرش عبده لأن الحر إن كان البادئ بالجناية يجب عليه جميع أرش عبده، وإن كان اللاحق فهو لا يجب عليه شيء فيجب نصفه، وإن شاء فداه بجميع أرش الحر ورجع على الحر بجميع أرش عبده لأنه يجب على الحر جميع أرش العبد تقدمت جنايته أو تأخرت فإن كانا سواء اتفقا، وإن كان أحدهما أقل فالأقل بمثله قصاصاً يصير من ويرد الفضل على صاحبه.
A free man and a slave met, each with a stick, and they fought each other, causing a scalp wound to each other. Then the slave's owner and the free man differed about who started. The statement is that of the owner: the free man started, and he owes the arsh of his injury to the slave to the owner, then he hands over the slave for his injury or ransoms him, because the free man acknowledged the arsh of the hand due to the injury, since he claimed immunity when the owner chose to hand over the slave to him, and the owner denied, so the owner's statement is taken. If the slave had a sword and the free man had a stick, then the slave died and the free man recovered, and they differed, the owner's statement is taken. The value of the slave is upon the free man's 'a'qila. The owner subtracts the amount by which the free man decreased the slave's value up to the day the slave struck the free man, and the rest is the value of the arsh of his injury upon the free man. If anything remains, it is for the owner, because the free man killed with a stick, so it is a killing resembling error (khata' al-'amd), so its value is due on the free man's 'a'qila. The value stands in place of the slave as if the slave were alive, so the owner takes the amount diminished by the free man's injury, and the free man takes from the remainder the arsh of his wound. If anything remains, it is for the owner, as it is the substitute for his slave, and the slave is free from the claim of others. If the remainder is insufficient, nothing is owed by the owner, just as if he handed over the slave and his value is less than the arsh of the wound. If the sword was with the free man and the slave had a stick, then the slave died and the free man recovered, and they do not know which of them started the injury, then the owner may kill the free man and the free man's right is void, because the free man killed with a sword intentionally, so retaliation (qisas) is due. The slave has died and left no substitute, so the free man's right becomes void. Similarly, if the slave was the one who started the injury, because it is unimaginable that the slave would acquire ownership after his death. If each had a stick and they gave each other a scalp wound (mudihah) and recovered, and they agree that they do not know who started, the owner has the option: if he hands over the slave, he may claim from the free man half the arsh of his slave, because if the free man was the initiator, he owes the full arsh of the slave; if he was the subsequent, he owes nothing, so half is due. If he prefers, he may ransom the slave by paying the full arsh of the free man, and then claim from the free man the full arsh of his slave, because the free man owes the full arsh of the slave whether his injury preceded or followed. If they are equal (the wounds are of the same type), they offset each other; if one is less, the lesser is offset by its equivalent in qisas, and the excess is returned to its owner.

Commentary
This passage presents several scenarios of mutual injuries between a free man and a slave, focusing on the determination of liability based on who started and the type of weapon used. The author rules that when both have sticks, the owner's statement is taken that the free man started, and the free man owes arsh for his own hand (in the initial scenario) and must either hand over the slave or ransom him. When the slave has a sword and the free man a stick, and the slave dies, the free man's 'a'qila pays the slave's value; the owner deducts the depreciation caused by the free man's injury, and the free man gets his arsh from the remainder. When the free man has a sword and the slave a stick, and the slave dies, the owner can execute qisas against the free man, voiding the free man's right because the slave is dead. Finally, when both have sticks and both receive mudihah, and the initiator is unknown, the owner may choose to hand over the slave and get half the slave's arsh from the free man, or ransom the slave by paying the free man's full arsh and then claim full arsh for his slave from the free man. The rulings are based on the principle that the law takes the statement of the one who has authority (the owner) and that the type of weapon classifies the homicide as 'amd (sword) or khata' al-'amd (stick).

Key Terms
موضحة (mudihah): A scalp wound that exposes the bone; its arsh is 1/20 of the full diyah.

Co-owners of a Slave: One Forgives Qisas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas forgiveness by co-owner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the effect of one co-owner forgiving qisas and distinguish between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's views.

Core Idea
When one co-owner forgives qisas, the whole right is void – Abu Hanifa; the other must be compensated – Abu Yusuf.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario: a slave jointly owned by two individuals kills a relative of both. One co-owner forgives the right to qisas. Does the other co-owner retain any claim? The Hanafi school presents a classic intra-madhhab dispute: Abu Hanifa rules that the entire qisas right becomes void, while his student Abu Yusuf argues that partial compensation is due. We will analyze their reasoning and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Abu Hanifa's Ruling
Recognize the facts: a jointly owned slave kills a relative of both owners. One co-owner forgives qisas. According to Abu Hanifa, the entire right to qisas is void. No additional claim arises against the slave.
This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's Counter-View
Abu Yusuf holds that forgiveness by one co-owner turns the qisas right into property. The forgiving co-owner must compensate the other by paying half his own share or ransoming the slave with one-fourth of the diyah.
The compensation is not the full value; it's calculated based on the division of the slave's value.
3
Trace the Reasoning of Each Imam
For Abu Yusuf: ownership does not preclude qisas; forgiveness converts part into property. Since a master cannot claim money from his own slave, only the portion corresponding to the other co-owner's share becomes due (one-fourth of diyah). For Abu Hanifa: when qisas becomes a monetary claim, it belongs to the deceased owner. The heirs stand in his place, but since the master could not claim money against his own slave, the claim fails entirely.
The key difference is over whether the right is divisible and whether the master's inability to claim against his own slave applies to the entire claim or only half.
4
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the forgiving co-owner retains his original ownership share without any further liability. Under Abu Yusuf, the forgiving co-owner must pay half his share or ransom with one-fourth of diyah.
This difference affects the rights and obligations of the co-owners after forgiveness.
5
Confirm the Mufta Bihi (Relied-Upon Position)
The Hanafi school adopts Abu Hanifa's view as the operative position for fatwa. This is confirmed by later authorities such as al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.
Abu Yusuf's view, though legitimate, is not applied in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (عبدهما قتل قريبهما فعفا أحدهما بطل الكل معناه إن كان عبد بين رجلين فقتل قريباً لهما كأمهما أولى أو أخيهما فعفا أحدهما بطل الجميع ولا يستحق غير العافي منهما شيئاً من العبد غير نصيبه الذي كان له من قبل، وكذا إذا كان العبد لقريب لهما وهو بربع الدية لأن القصاص ثبت لهما حق أو لمعتقهما فقتل مولاه فورثاه بطل الكل هذا عند أبي حنيفة وقال أبو يوسف : يدفع الذي عفا نصف نصيبه إلى الآخر إن شاء، وإن شاء فداه في العبد على الشيوع لأن الملك لا ينافي استحقاق القصاص عليه للمولى فإذا عفا أحدهما انقلب نصيب الآخر النصف مالاً . أنه غير شائع في كل العبد فيكون نصفه في نصيبه ونصفه في نصيب صاحبه فما أصاب نصيبه سقط لأن المولى لا يستوجب على عبده مالاً، وما أصاب نصيب صاحبه ثبت وهو نصف النصف وهو الربع فيدفع نصف نصيبه أو يفديه بربع الدية. ولأبي حنيفة أن ما يجب من المال يكون حق المولى لأنه بدل دمه ولهذا يقضى منه ديونه وتنفذ منه ،وصاياه ثم الورثة يخلفونه فيه عند الفراغ من حاجته والمولى لا يستوجب على عبده مالاً فلا تخلفه الورثة فيه، ولأن القصاص لما صار مالاً صار بمعنى الخطأ وفيه لا يجب شيء فكذا ما هو في معنى ذلك.
He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'If a slave belonging to both of them kills their relative, and one of them forgives, the whole (right to qisas) is void.' Its meaning is: if a slave common between two men kills a relative of both, such as their mother or father or brother, and one of them forgives, the entire (right) is void. No one except the one who forgave gets anything from the slave other than his share that he had before. Similarly, if the slave is a relative of both and it is one-fourth of the diyah, because qisas was established as a right for them, or if the slave is their freed slave and he kills his master, so they inherit him, the whole is void. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: The one who forgave may pay half of his share to the other if he wishes, or if he wishes, he may ransom the slave based on common ownership, because ownership does not preclude the right of qisas against him for the owner. When one of them forgives, the share of the other turns into half of the property. It (the half) is not undivided in the entire slave, so half of it is in his share and half is in the share of his companion. What falls on his share is dropped because the owner cannot claim money against his own slave, and what falls on the share of his companion is established, which is half of the half, i.e., one-fourth. So he pays half of his share or ransoms him with one-fourth of the diyah. For Abu Hanifa, what becomes due of money is the right of the owner, because it is the substitute for his blood. For this reason, debts are paid from it and his bequests are executed. Then the heirs succeed him in it after his needs are satisfied. But the owner cannot claim money against his own slave, so the heirs cannot succeed him in that. Also, because when qisas turns into money, it becomes like an error, and in error nothing is due (from the slave's owner), so what is in that meaning is similar.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of one co-owner forgiving qisas. Abu Hanifa holds that forgiveness by one co-owner completely voids the right to qisas, and the other co-owner gets nothing additional; they only retain their original ownership share. Abu Yusuf disagrees: the forgiving co-owner must compensate the other by paying half of his share or ransoming the slave with one-fourth of the diyah, because the forgiveness turns part of the qisas into property. The text provides Abu Yusuf's reasoning in detail: the qisas right is divisible, and since a master cannot owe money to himself, only the portion corresponding to the non-forgiving co-owner's share becomes due. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that when qisas becomes a monetary claim, it is the right of the deceased owner, and the heirs stand in his place, but a master cannot claim money against his own slave, so the claim fails. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view on this issue, as explicitly stated in the text.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliatory justice, the right of the victim's heirs to demand execution or pardon.
الشيوع (al-shuyu'): Common ownership, undivided shares in a single property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when one co-owner of a slave forgives qisas for the slave killing their relative?
A) The other co-owner gets half the slave's value
B) The entire right to qisas is void
C) The forgiving co-owner must pay the other half his share
D) The slave is set free

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that forgiveness by one co-owner of a slave voids the entire right to qisas, leaving the other co-owner with no additional claim.

Diyah Liability After Cutting and Forgiveness
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah after cutting and forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on diyah liability when a victim's heir cuts the killer's hand and then forgives, and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's view and that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Forgiveness after cutting does not erase liability if the cut was separate from qisas execution.

In this passage from al-Kafi, we encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute about the intersection of qisas and diyah. The scenario involves a heir who cuts the hand of the murderer, then forgives. Does the heir owe diyah for the hand? Abu Hanifa says yes; the Sahibayn say no. Understanding their reasoning requires careful analysis of when the cutting occurred relative to forgiveness and whether it was part of the qisas execution.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and rulings
Identify that the heir cuts the killer's hand and then forgives. Abu Hanifa: diyah wajib; Sahibayn: no liability.
The key is whether the cut was independent or part of qisas.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
He views the cut as an independent injury because forgiveness occurred before the execution of qisas was complete.
The cut is not covered by later forgiveness.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
They see the cut as part of the qisas execution, so forgiveness afterwards does not create liability. They use an analogy to cutting fingers after forgiving the hand.
The analogy supports their position.
4
Consider the additional cases
The text discusses wound spread, healing, and beheading. In each, the disagreement remains or is resolved by the same principles.
Practice tracing these cases.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa the heir pays diyah; under the Sahibayn he pays nothing.
This affects fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكافي : ومن قتل وليه عمداً فقطع يد قاتله ثم عفا وقد قضى له بالقصاص أو لم يقض فعلى قاطع اليد دية عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا شيء عليه. وكذا إذا عفا ثم سرى لا والقطع الساري أفحش من المقتصر وصار كما لو كان يضمن شيئاً له قصاص في اليد فقطع أصابعه ثم عفا عن اليد فإنه لا يضمن أرش الأصابع والأصابع والكف كأطراف النفس. ولو قطع وما عفا ثم برأ فهو على الخلاف في الصحيح، ولو قطع ثم حز رقبته قبل البرء فهو على استيفاء قتل يضمن حتى لو حز رقبته بعد البرء فهو على الخلاف في الصحيح.
And in al-Kafi: Whoever kills his relative intentionally, and then (the victim's heir) cuts off the hand of the killer, then forgives, whether qisas had been decreed or not, according to Abu Hanifa, the one who cut the hand owes diyah (for the hand). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: nothing is due on him. Similarly, if he forgives then (the wound) spreads, and the cutting that spreads is more severe than the limited cutting, and it becomes like one who is liable for something that has qisas in the hand, so he cuts his fingers, then forgives the hand; he does not guarantee the arsh of the fingers. The fingers and the palm are like the limbs of the self. If he cuts and does not forgive, then (the wound) heals, it is according to the disagreement on the correct view. If he cuts then cuts his neck before healing, it is in execution of a killing, and he is liable. If he cuts his neck after healing, it is according to the disagreement on the correct view.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage from al-Kafi (a Hanafi text) presents a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a scenario where the heir of a murdered person cuts the killer's hand, then forgives. Abu Hanifa holds that the heir owes diyah for the hand he cut, because his act of cutting was an independent injury. The Two say no diyah is due, because the cutting was part of executing qisas for the murder, and forgiveness after cutting does not create liability. The text also discusses cases where the cut spreads to cause more harm, where the cut heals, and where the killer is beheaded. The principle is that if the cut is part of the qisas execution, later forgiveness does not create liability unless the cut was separate from the execution. This shows the nuance within the Hanafi school regarding the intersection of qisas and diyah.

Key Terms
الساري (al-sari): A wound that spreads, causing additional damage beyond the initial cut.
دية (diyah): Monetary compensation for bodily harm or homicide.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment; in this context, cutting the killer's hand as retribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the timing of forgiveness relative to the act of cutting determines whether diyah is due; Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on whether the cut is part of qisas execution or an independent injury.

Two Wounds: One Forgiven, One Not, Death Ensues
Ruling on diyah for combined wounds with partial forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah liability when two intentional scalp wounds (mudihah), one forgiven and one not, cause death, and explain why forgiveness only voids qisas not the monetary claim.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of a wound waives qisas but not diyah; full diyah is due when death results from multiple wounds.

This lesson deals with a specific scenario in diyat law: an assailant inflicts two intentional scalp wounds, forgives the first, then the victim dies from both. The ruling clarifies how forgiveness interacts with liability and why full diyah is still due. It also highlights a riwayah from Abu Yusuf confirming the same position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Read the scenario: two intentional scalp wounds (mudihah), the first forgiven, the second not forgiven, victim dies from both.
2
Understand the effect of forgiveness
Forgiveness of the first wound drops qisas for that wound but does not drop the diyah claim; it becomes a monetary claim.
3
Recognize the combined effect
Since both wounds caused death, the assailant is liable for full diyah, not just for the second wound.
4
Note the modality
The diyah is wajib and is to be paid in three years, as is standard for diyah in cases resembling intentional harm.
5
Observe the riwayah
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Yusuf that the same ruling applies, confirming consistency in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً فعفا عنها وما يحدث منها ثم شجه شجة أخرى عمداً فلم يعف عنه فعلى الجاني الدية كاملة في ثلاث سنين إذا مات منها جميعاً من قبل الأولى بطل عنه القصاص وصارت الثانية مالاً وصارت الأولى أيضاً مالاً ولم يجز له العفو لأنه لا وصية له، وروى الحسن بن زياد عن أبي يوسف في مثل هذه الصورة أن على الجاني الدية .
He wounded a man's scalp (mudihah) intentionally, and forgave that and what might arise from it. Then he wounded him another intentional scalp wound, and did not forgive it. Then the full diyah is due on the assailant in three years, if he dies from both together. Regarding the first, qisas is dropped, and the second becomes a monetary claim, and the first also becomes a monetary claim, and it is not permissible for him to forgive because he has no bequest (will). And al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Yusuf in such a case that the diyah is due on the assailant.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is a ruling on a specific scenario: a person intentionally inflicts a scalp wound (mudihah) on another, and then forgives that wound and any subsequent complications from it. Later, he inflicts a second intentional scalp wound without forgiving it. The victim dies as a result of both wounds. The ruling: the full diyah is due on the assailant, to be paid in three years (as is usual for diyah in cases resembling intentional harm). The first wound's forgiveness voids qisas for that wound, but since it was not completely separate, it converts to a monetary claim. The second wound also converts to a monetary claim. The victim cannot execute qisas because he had already forgiven the first, so only diyah remains. The note from Hasan ibn Ziyad quoting Abu Yusuf confirms the same ruling. The mention that he cannot forgive because he has no bequest appears to be a side note: since the right to diyah is inherited, he cannot forgive after death.

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A scalp wound that exposes the bone; a specific type of injury with a fixed arsh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person inflicts two intentional scalp wounds, forgives the first, and the victim dies from both, what is the liability?
A) Qisas for the second wound only
B) Full diyah in three years
C) Half diyah only
D) Nothing because the first was forgiven

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness of a specific wound only waives qisas, not diyah; when death results from multiple injuries, full diyah is wajib.

Liability When an Executor Kills After Forgiveness
Ruling on diyah liability when executor kills after forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on diyah liability when a qisas executor kills after the heir's forgiveness.

Core Idea
The heir who orders qisas bears diyah if the executor kills in ignorance of forgiveness.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in qisas: after an heir has ordered the execution, he forgives the killer but the appointed executor, unaware of the forgiveness, proceeds to kill. The Hanafi ruling holds the heir liable for diyah. Understanding this ruling clarifies how revocation of permission interacts with liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the killing by the appointed executor. The ruling is that diyah is wajib (obligatory) upon the heir who gave the order, not upon the executor. This is because the executor acted under the original valid order and was unaware of the forgiveness.
The liability is on the one who caused the execution, not the unwitting agent.
2
Identify the parties involved
Three parties: the original killer (victim of qisas), the heir who has the right of qisas, and the appointed executor (ma'mur). The heir forgives the killer after ordering the execution.
The heir's forgiveness revokes the permission to kill.
3
Identify the key condition: ignorance of the executor
The executor's lack of knowledge is critical. If he had known of the forgiveness, he would be criminally liable. Since he acted in good faith based on the original order, his act is not considered murder; the heir's order is the cause.
Ignorance of the executor protects him from liability but transfers it to the heir.
4
Derive the liability
The diyah is wajib on the heir because his order was the direct cause of the killing, and he failed to inform the executor of the forgiveness. The executor is not liable because he acted under a valid order that was only later revoked without his knowledge.
This ruling aligns with the Hanafi principle that an agent is not liable for following a principal's valid order until revocation is communicated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل قتل عمداً وقضى لوليه بالقصاص على القاتل فأمر الولي رجلاً بقتله ثم إنه طلب من الولي أن يعفو عن القاتل فعفا عنه فقتله المأمور وهو لا يعلم بالعفو قال : عليه الدية بذلك على الآمر
A man was killed intentionally, and qisas was decreed for his heir against the killer. The heir ordered a man to kill him (the killer). Then he (someone) asked the heir to forgive the killer, and he forgave him. Then the appointed man killed him while he (the appointed) did not know of the forgiveness. He (the author) said: the diyah is due for that upon the one who ordered (the heir).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling: if the heir orders someone to execute qisas, then later forgives the killer, but the appointed executor, unaware of the forgiveness, proceeds to kill, the heir is liable for diyah. This is because the permission to kill was revoked by the forgiveness, and the appointed executor acted under the original order which was no longer valid. The diyah is not on the executor (since he acted in good faith) but on the heir who gave the now-invalid order.

Key Terms
المأمور (al-ma'mur): The appointed person who carries out the qisas execution.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid as compensation for unlawful killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario from the passage, who is liable to pay diyah?
A) The appointed executor (ma'mur)
B) The heir who ordered the execution
C) The original killer's family
D) No one, because qisas was due

Key Takeaway
When an heir forgives after ordering qisas but the executor kills unaware, the heir must pay diyah.

Woman Kills Man Unintentionally, Marries His Heir on Diyah
A ruling on the validity of a marriage involving diyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the marriage of an accidental killer to the victim's heir on the basis of diyah and explain the effect on the 'a'qila's liability.

Core Idea
Marriage can validly include a claim for diyah, discharging the 'a'qila, but divorce before consummation revives half the claim.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific intersection of diyat (blood money) and nikah (marriage). When a woman accidentally kills a man, the diyah is due on her 'a'qila. If the victim's heir marries her, agreeing to take the diyah as part of the marriage contract, the marriage is valid and the 'a'qila is freed. However, if the marriage ends before consummation, the original diyah obligation partially returns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
A woman kills a man accidentally, so diyah is due on her 'a'qila (clan).
2
Explain the marriage arrangement
The heir of the slain man marries the woman on the basis of the diyah, meaning he claims the diyah from the 'a'qila as part of the marriage contract.
3
State the ruling on the marriage
The marriage is permissible (ja'iz), and the 'a'qila is discharged from its obligation.
4
Analyze the divorce scenario
If the husband divorces her before consummation, he can claim half the diyah from the 'a'qila.
The original obligation revives partially.
5
Summarize the legal effect
The marriage contract transfers the diyah claim to the husband; the 'a'qila's liability is conditional on the marriage's completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امرأة قتلت رجلاً خطأ فتزوجها ولي المقتول على الدية ويرجع التي وجبت على العاقلة فذلك جائز والعاقلة براء، فإن طلقها قبل الدخول بها رجع على العاقلة بنصف الدية
A woman killed a man accidentally. The heir of the slain man married her on the basis of the diyah. He claims the diyah that was due on the 'a'qila. That is permissible, and the 'a'qila is discharged. If he divorces her before consummation, he can claim from the 'a'qila half of the diyah.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This ruling addresses a unique case: a woman accidentally kills a man, and the heir of the victim marries her, with the understanding that the diyah (blood money) is part of the marriage contract. The diyah was originally due on the 'a'qila (the woman's clan). By marrying her, the heir takes on the claim for diyah, and the 'a'qila is freed from liability. If the heir divorces her before consummation, he can recover half of the diyah from the 'a'qila because the marriage did not proceed, and the original obligation revives partially. This is based on the principle that a valid marriage contract can include terms about financial obligations.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'a'qila): The clan or group responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman accidentally kills a man and the heir marries her on the basis of diyah, what happens to the 'a'qila's liability?
A) They remain liable for full diyah
B) They are discharged from liability
C) They are liable for half if divorce occurs before consummation
D) Both B and C are correct

Key Takeaway
A marriage contract can validly include a claim for diyah, discharging the 'a'qila, but divorce before consummation revives half the diyah claim against them.

Liability for Qisas in Joint Scalp Wounds
Ruling when two intentional scalp wounds cause death, only latter is liable for qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas liability when two intentional scalp wounds cause death and identify the effect of a prior settlement.

Core Idea
Only the latter assailant faces qisas when two wounds cause death; the first is exonerated.

This lesson addresses a scenario where two assailants each inflict a scalp wound, and the victim dies from the combination. The Hanafi school assigns liability to the second assailant alone. We will unpack the rationale and see how a settlement with the first does not affect the latter's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled? Two separate intentional scalp wounds (mudihah amdan) by two different persons. Ruling: qisas wajib on the latter, nothing on the first.
2
Locate the operative principle
The latter is liable because his wound was the immediate cause of death. The first wound alone was not fatal.
This highlights Hanafi causation (sababiyyah).
3
Analyze the effect of settlement
If the first assailant makes a settlement (sulh) with the victim after the second wound, the latter remains liable. Sulh binds only the parties to it.
Abu al-Fadl notes istihsan supports this.
4
Note the epistemological weight
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, the core Hanafi texts, and is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً ومات من الموضحتين فعلى الآخر القصاص ولا شيء على الأول، وكذلك لو كان الصلح مع الأول بعد ما شجه الآخر قال أبو الفضل : فقد استحسن في موضع آخر من هذا الكتاب أنه له القصاص على الآخر إذا كان شجه بعد صلح الأول.
A man wounded another with an intentional scalp wound (mudihah), and he died from both scalp wounds (i.e., from two separate assailants). The latter (assailant) is subject to qisas, and nothing is due on the first. Similarly, if there was a settlement (sulh) with the first after the other wounded him, Abu al-Fadl said: he (the author) considered it good (istahsana) in another place of this book that he has qisas against the latter if he wounded after the settlement with the first.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This ruling addresses multiple assailants: if two people each inflict an intentional scalp wound, and the victim dies from the combination, the liability falls on the second assailant, not the first. This is because the first wound alone was not fatal, and the second wound tipped the balance. If the first assailant made a settlement (sulh) with the victim after the second wound, Abu al-Fadl mentions that the author (implied: al-Marghinani or the original author of Kunz) considered it good (istihsan) that the victim still has qisas against the latter assailant, since the settlement does not exonerate the latter. This shows the nuance of causative liability in joint injuries.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A settlement agreement between the victim and the assailant, typically involving payment in lieu of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two people each give an intentional scalp wound and the victim dies from both, who is liable for qisas?
A) Both equally
B) The first assailant
C) The latter assailant
D) No one

Key Takeaway
The latter assailant alone is liable for qisas when two intentional scalp wounds cause death, and a settlement with the first does not change that.

Multiple Wounds and Pardon: Full Diyah for Two Mudihah
Ruling on multiple wounds with pardon and subsequent death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mudihah is pardoned and a second mudihah is inflicted without pardon, leading to death from both.

Core Idea
Pardoning a wound removes qisas but not liability; a later wound makes both monetary, requiring full diyah.

This passage addresses a common scenario in diyat: an initial mudihah is pardoned, yet a second wound occurs. If death results from both, the injurer's liability transforms. Understanding this clarifies how pardon interacts with multiple injuries and the obligation of diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Two intentional mudihah wounds are inflicted; the first is pardoned, the second is not. If the victim dies from both, the injurer must pay full diyah in three years.
2
Analyze Effect of Pardon on Qisas
The pardon of the first wound cancels the right to qisas for that wound, but it does not extinguish liability entirely; it converts the wound into a monetary obligation.
3
Determine Liability for Combined Death
Since the second wound is unpardoned, qisas is still possible for it, but because the victim died from both wounds, qisas cannot be applied partially. Therefore, both wounds become monetary, and full diyah is due.
The pardon of the first wound is retroactively invalidated because it was conditional on no further harm.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Difference
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reports from Abu Yusuf that full diyah is due, agreeing with the main ruling, but he differs on the reasoning or scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً فعفا عنها وما يحدث منها ثم شجه شجة أخرى عمداً فلم يعف عنه فعلى الجاني الدية كاملة في ثلاث سنين إذا مات منها جميعاً من قبل الأولى بطل عنه القصاص وصارت الثانية مالاً وصارت الأولى أيضاً مالاً ولم يجز له العفو لأنه لا وصية له، وروى الحسن بن زياد عن أبي يوسف في مثل هذه الصورة أن على الجاني الدية . رجل قتل عمداً وقضى لوليه بالقصاص على القاتل فأمر الولي رجلاً بقتله ثم إنه طلب من الولي أن يعفو عن القاتل فعفا عنه فقتله المأمور وهو لا يعلم بالعفو قال : عليه الدية بذلك على الآمر امرأة قتلت رجلاً خطأ فتزوجها ولي المقتول على الدية ويرجع التي وجبت على العاقلة فذلك جائز والعاقلة براء، فإن طلقها قبل الدخول بها رجع على العاقلة بنصف الدية رجل شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً ومات من الموضحتين فعلى الآخر القصاص ولا شيء على الأول، وكذلك لو كان الصلح مع الأول بعد ما شجه الآخر قال أبو الفضل : فقد استحسن في موضع آخر من هذا الكتاب أنه له القصاص على الآخر إذا كان شجه بعد صلح الأول. رجل شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً وصالحه عنها وما يحدث منها على أنه عفا عن
A man struck another causing a scalp wound (mudihah) intentionally and pardoned it and whatever may result from it, then he struck him another strike intentionally and did not pardon; upon the injurer is full blood money payable in three years if he died from both [wounds]. Because the first [wound] removes qisas and the second becomes money, and the first also becomes money, and his pardon is not valid because he has no will [for it?]. And it is narrated by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Yusuf in such a case that upon the injurer is the blood money. A man killed intentionally and the guardian obtained a verdict of qisas against the killer; then the guardian ordered a man to kill him; then he asked the guardian to pardon the killer and he did, but the ordered man killed him without knowing of the pardon. He said: Upon the commander is the blood money for that. A woman killed a man by mistake, and the guardian of the deceased married her on the basis of the blood money, and that [blood money] which was due on the 'aqilah is returned? That is permissible, and the 'aqilah are free. If he divorced her before consummation, he shall claim from the 'aqilah half the blood money. A man struck another a scalp wound intentionally and he died from the two scalp wounds; upon the other [?] is qisas and nothing upon the first. Likewise if a settlement was made with the first after the other struck him. Abu al-Fadl said: He has ruled good in another place of this book that he has qisas against the other if he struck after the first's settlement. A man struck another a scalp wound intentionally and settled with him for it and whatever may result from it on the condition that he pardoned...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings related to multiple intentional wounds (mudihah) and the effect of pardon. The first case: a man inflicts a mudihah (a scalp wound exposing bone) intentionally. He pardons the first wound and any future consequences. Then he strikes a second mudihah without pardon. If the victim dies from the combination of both wounds, the injurer must pay full diyah (blood money) in three years (installments). The reasoning: the first wound, being pardoned, removes the right to qisas, so it becomes a monetary liability. The second wound, for which no pardon was given, also becomes money because qisas is now impossible (since the first wound already converted to money). However, the pardon of the first wound is invalidated because the pardoning party cannot have a will for future wounds? The text says 'لا وصية له' meaning he has no will (testament) for that, so the pardon is not effective? Actually, it might mean that he cannot make a will for something that is not yet in existence. This is a disputed point. According to a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Yusuf, the full diyah is due. Next, a case of intentional killing where the guardian orders someone to execute the qisas, then pardons the killer, but the executor kills without knowledge of the pardon. The executor is liable for diyah, and it falls on the commander (the guardian) because he gave the order. Another case: a woman kills a man by mistake, and the victim's guardian marries her in lieu of the diyah. The diyah that had been due on the 'aqilah (the killer's tribe) is cancelled; the marriage is valid, and the 'aqilah are freed from the diyah. If he divorces her before consummation, he can claim half the diyah from the 'aqilah. Next: two men inflict mudihah on a third, and he dies from both. Only the one who struck second is liable for qisas; the first is free. If there was a settlement with the first before the second struck, the second is still liable. Abu al-Fadl notes that in another part of this book, the author ruled that if the second struck after a settlement with the first, the second is subject to qisas. The passage ends mid-sentence. The authority is from later fatawa (Abu al-Fadl).

Key Terms
موضحة (mudihah): A scalp wound that exposes the bone (cranial wound).
دية (diyah): Blood money for wounds or killing, here payable in three years.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario of two mudihah wounds where the first is pardoned and the second is not, and the victim dies from both, what is the liability?
A) Only the first wound is pardoned, so no liability.
B) Only the second wound is liable for qisas.
C) The injurer must pay full diyah in three years.
D) The injurer must pay half diyah for each wound.

Key Takeaway
Pardoning a wound removes qisas but not monetary liability; if a subsequent wound causes death, both wounds become subject to diyah.

Ordered Execution After Pardon: Liability of the Commander
Ruling on ordered execution after pardon where executor unaware

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a guardian orders execution of qisas, then pardons the killer, but the executioner kills unaware of the pardon.

Core Idea
If a guardian orders execution and later pardons, but the executioner kills before learning of the pardon, the diyah is on the commander.

This passage deals with a situation where a verdict of qisas is obtained, but the guardian pardons the killer after ordering execution. If the executioner carries out the order without knowledge, the liability shifts to the commander. This highlights the importance of communication and the effect of pardon on pending orders.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sequence of Events
The guardian obtains a qisas verdict and orders a man to kill the convicted killer. Then the guardian pardons the killer, but the ordered man kills without knowing of the pardon.
2
Identify the Legal Issue
Who is liable for the killing? The executor acted on a valid order but the order was revoked by the pardon. The executor believed the order was still in effect.
3
State the Ruling
The diyah (blood money) is due on the commander (the guardian) because the executor was acting under his authority and was not informed of the revocation.
4
Differentiate from Other Scenarios
If the executor had known of the pardon, he would be liable for intentional killing. Here, his ignorance mitigates his liability, shifting it to the one who gave the order and then pardoned without retracting the order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The reasoning: the first wound, being pardoned, removes the right to qisas, so it becomes a monetary liability. The second wound, for which no pardon was given, also becomes money because qisas is now impossible (since the first wound already converted to money). However, the pardon of the first wound is invalidated because the pardoning party cannot have a will for future wounds? The text says 'لا وصية له' meaning he has no will (testament) for that, so the pardon is not effective? Actually, it might mean that he cannot make a will for something that is not yet in existence. This is a disputed point. According to a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Yusuf, the full diyah is due. Next, a case of intentional killing where the guardian orders someone to execute the qisas, then pardons the killer, but the executor kills without knowledge of the pardon. The executor is liable for diyah, and it falls on the commander (the guardian) because he gave the order. Another case: a woman kills a man by mistake, and the victim's guardian marries her in lieu of the diyah. The diyah that had been due on the 'aqilah (the killer's tribe) is cancelled; the marriage is valid, and the 'aqilah are freed from the diyah. If he divorces her before consummation, he can claim half the diyah from the 'aqilah. Next: two men inflict mudihah on a third, and he dies from both. Only the one who struck second is liable for qisas; the first is free. If there was a settlement with the first before the second struck, the second is still liable. Abu al-Fadl notes that in another part of this book, the author ruled that if the second struck after a settlement with the first, the second is subject to qisas. The passage ends mid-sentence. The authority is from later fatawa (Abu al-Fadl).

Key Terms
ولي (wali): Guardian of the victim, here the one entitled to qisas.
عفو (afw): Pardon, which remits the right to qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ordered execution case, who pays the diyah when the executor is unaware of the pardon?
A) The executor
B) The commander (guardian)
C) No one, because the pardon was effective
D) The convicted killer's family

Key Takeaway
Pardon must be communicated; if an order to execute remains uncancelled, the commander bears the diyah for the killing.

Diyat — Blood Money

Marriage in Lieu of Diyah and Two Attacker Liability
Rulings on marriage settling diyah and two attackers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a killer marries the heir in settlement of diyah, and when two attackers inflict mudihah but only the second is liable for qisas.

Core Idea
Marriage can discharge diyah; divorce before consummation revives half the claim. With two attackers, only the second who struck unpardoned is liable for qisas.

This lesson covers two distinct rulings from the passage: first, a woman who accidentally kills a man may marry the heir and thereby settle the diyah, but if divorced before consummation, half the diyah returns to the 'aqilah. Second, if two men each inflict a mudihah and the victim dies, only the second attacker is liable for qisas because the first had already settled or was pardoned. These illustrate how liability can be transferred or split in diyat law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Marriage-in-Lieu Scenario
A woman kills a man by mistake; the diyah is due on her 'aqilah. The victim's heir marries her on the condition that the marriage cancels the diyah. This is permissible; the 'aqilah are freed.
2
Examine Post-Divorce Liability
If the husband divorces her before consummating the marriage, he can claim half the diyah from the 'aqilah, because the marriage was a full settlement but divorce ends it partially.
3
Analyze the Two-Attacker Case
Two men each inflict a mudihah on a third person. If the victim dies from both wounds, only the one who struck the second wound (without pardon or settlement) is liable for qisas; the first is free because his liability was already resolved.
4
Note the Extension with Settlement
If the first attacker settled with the victim (e.g., paid diyah) and then the second struck, the second is still liable for qisas. The settlement does not protect the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً فعفا عنها وما يحدث منها ثم شجه شجة أخرى عمداً فلم يعف عنه فعلى الجاني الدية كاملة في ثلاث سنين إذا مات منها جميعاً من قبل الأولى بطل عنه القصاص وصارت الثانية مالاً وصارت الأولى أيضاً مالاً ولم يجز له العفو لأنه لا وصية له، وروى الحسن بن زياد عن أبي يوسف في مثل هذه الصورة أن على الجاني الدية . رجل قتل عمداً وقضى لوليه بالقصاص على القاتل فأمر الولي رجلاً بقتله ثم إنه طلب من الولي أن يعفو عن القاتل فعفا عنه فقتله المأمور وهو لا يعلم بالعفو قال : عليه الدية بذلك على الآمر امرأة قتلت رجلاً خطأ فتزوجها ولي المقتول على الدية ويرجع التي وجبت على العاقلة فذلك جائز والعاقلة براء، فإن طلقها قبل الدخول بها رجع على العاقلة بنصف الدية رجل شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً ومات من الموضحتين فعلى الآخر القصاص ولا شيء على الأول، وكذلك لو كان الصلح مع الأول بعد ما شجه الآخر قال أبو الفضل : فقد استحسن في موضع آخر من هذا الكتاب أنه له القصاص على الآخر إذا كان شجه بعد صلح الأول. رجل شج رجلاً موضحة عمداً وصالحه عنها وما يحدث منها على أنه عفا عن
A man struck another causing a scalp wound (mudihah) intentionally and pardoned it and whatever may result from it, then he struck him another strike intentionally and did not pardon; upon the injurer is full blood money payable in three years if he died from both [wounds]. A man killed intentionally and the guardian obtained a verdict of qisas against the killer; then the guardian ordered a man to kill him; then he asked the guardian to pardon the killer and he did, but the ordered man killed him without knowing of the pardon. He said: Upon the commander is the blood money for that. Commentary
This passage presents several rulings related to multiple intentional wounds (mudihah) and the effect of pardon. The first case: a man inflicts a mudihah (a scalp wound exposing bone) intentionally. He pardons the first wound and any future consequences. Then he strikes a second mudihah without pardon. If the victim dies from the combination of both wounds, the injurer must pay full diyah (blood money) in three years (installments). The reasoning: the first wound, being pardoned, removes the right to qisas, so it becomes a monetary liability. The second wound, for which no pardon was given, also becomes money because qisas is now impossible (since the first wound already converted to money). However, the pardon of the first wound is invalidated because the pardoning party cannot have a will for future wounds? The executor is liable for diyah, and it falls on the commander (the guardian) because he gave the order. The authority is from later fatawa (Abu al-Fadl).

Key Terms
عاقلة (aqilah): The tribe or group of male relatives who pay diyah on behalf of the killer in cases of manslaughter.
صلح (sulh): Settlement, a contract to resolve a dispute, here used to discharge liability for a wound.

C) Only the second attacker is liable.
D) Neither is liable because death from wounds is not intentional.

Key Takeaway
Settlement or pardon of one attacker removes him from qisas liability; a subsequent attacker remains fully responsible.

Diyah for a Slave Killed by Mistake: Capped Value vs. Full Value
Ruling with proof on capped diyah for accidental killing of a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for a slave killed by mistake, distinguish between the capped value and the full value positions, and explain the underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
For a slave killed accidentally, Hanafi law caps diyah at 10,000 dirhams (male) and 5,000 (female); full value applies only if the slave was usurped.

This lesson examines the diyah (blood money) for a slave killed accidentally. The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, imposes a maximum compensation of 10,000 dirhams for a male slave and 5,000 for a female, whereas Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that the full market value is due. The passage also clarifies that for a usurped slave, the full value is agreed upon. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the distinction between compensation for property and compensation for blood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (killing a slave by mistake) and state the ruling: diyah is wajib, but with a cap for regular accidental killing, and full value for a usurped slave.
The cap applies to accidental killing; full value for usurped slave by consensus.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the Quranic verse (4:92) and the practice of Caliphs 'Umar, 'Ali, and Ibn 'Umar. Also note the analogical reasoning comparing the slave to other property.
The Quranic verse mentions 'diyah musallamah' which Hanafis interpret as a monetary compensation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the evidence proves the ruling: the compensation is for property (value), not for blood, because it goes to the master and not the slave. Thus, it follows the rules of property guarantees.
The slave is considered free in matters of blood, so diyah cannot be blood money; it is property compensation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the dissenting positions: Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that full value is due without cap, based on the actions of the Caliphs.
This is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position) in the Hanafi school: the capped amount from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the relied-upon view.
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq adopts the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: عبد قتل خطأ تجب قيمته ونقص عشرة لو كانت عشرة آلاف أو أكثر وفي الأمة عشرة من خمسة آلاف وفي المغصوب تجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي في القن : تجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت وفي الغصب تجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت بالإجماع. لما روي عن عمر وعلي وابن عمر رضي الله عنهم أنهم أوجبوا في قتل العبد قيمته بالغة ما بلغت لأن الضمان بدل المالية ولهذا يجب للمولى وهو لا يملك إلا من حيث المالية، ولو كان بدل الدم لكان للعبد إذ هو وفي حق الدم مبقي على أصل الحرية فعلم أنه بدل المالية، ولهذا لو قتل العبد المبيع قبل القبض يبقى عقد البيع وبقاؤه ببقاء المالية أصلاً أو بدلاً في حال قيامه أو هلاكه فصار كسائر الأموال وكقليل القيمة والغصب ولأن ضمان المال بالمال أصل وضمان ما ليس بمال بالمال خلاف الأصل، ومهما أمكن إيجاب الضمان على موافقة القياس لا يصار إلى ايجابه بخلاف الاصل . قال القدوري في كتابه التقرير قال أبو يوسف إذا قتل المبيع في يد البائع فاختار المشتري إجازة البيع كان له القصاص، وكذا إن اختار فسخ البيع كان للبائع القصاص وهذا حفطي عن أبي حنيفة . قال أبو يوسف : ليس للبائع القصاص. وروى ابن زياد عنه لا قصاص للمشتري أيضاً. ولأبي حنيفة قوله تعالى {ودية مسلمة} [النساء : 92] أوجبها
Chapter: A slave killed by mistake: his value is obligatory and a reduction of ten if it was ten thousand or more. For a female slave, ten from five thousand. For a usurped slave, his value is obligatory however much it may be. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i in al-Qunn? said: His value is obligatory however much it may be. And for usurped, his value is obligatory however much it may be by consensus. Narrated from 'Umar, 'Ali, and Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, that they made obligatory in killing a slave his value however much it may be, because the guarantee is a substitute for monetary value, and therefore it is due to the master, and he does not own except in terms of monetary value. If it were a substitute for blood, it would belong to the slave because he, in the matter of blood, is preserved on the original freedom. Thus it is known that it is a substitute for monetary value. For that reason, if a sold slave is killed before delivery, the contract of sale remains, and its continuation is by continuation of the monetary value, originally or as a substitute in the state of its existence or destruction. So it becomes like other property, like of small value and usurped. And because guaranteeing property with property is the principle, and guaranteeing what is not property with property is against the principle. When it is possible to impose guarantee in conformity with analogy, we do not resort to imposing it contrary to the principle. Al-Quduri said in his book al-Taqrir: Abu Yusuf said: If the sold good is killed in the hands of the seller and the buyer chooses to validate the sale, he has the qisas. Likewise if he chooses to rescind the sale, the seller has the qisas. This is preserved from Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: The seller does not have qisas. Ibn Ziyad narrated from him that there is no qisas for the buyer either. Abu Hanifa [has] the statement of Allah the Exalted: {And a blood money delivered} [4:92] He made it obligatory.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This chapter discusses the value (diyah) for a slave killed by mistake. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) caps the compensation: for a male slave, the value is up to 10,000 dirhams; if the slave is worth more, the compensation is reduced by ten (i.e., only 10,000 is due). For a female slave, the cap is 5,000 dirhams. For a usurped slave (e.g., a stolen slave killed by someone other than the thief), the full value is due without cap. Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that the full value is due regardless of the amount. The proof cited is the practice of Caliphs 'Umar, 'Ali, and Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). Their reasoning: the compensation is a substitute for monetary value, not for blood, because the slave is property. Therefore, the owner is compensated for the loss of property value. If it were blood money, it would belong to the slave himself (since he is considered free in terms of blood), but since it goes to the master, it confirms it is a property guarantee. This is analogous to other property: if a sold item is killed before delivery, the buyer can still enforce the sale because the value persists. The text then quotes al-Quduri regarding a different scenario: if a sold slave is killed in the seller's hands, the buyer may choose to uphold the sale and have qisas, or rescind and the seller has qisas. But Abu Yusuf disagrees that the seller has qisas. This shows that the right to qisas for a slave is tied to ownership. The authority is from Zahir al-Riwayah? The chapter title and base ruling seem to be from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq), so the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah for the basic ruling, but the quotes from al-Quduri are later.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money for a slave, capped by value.
مغصوب (maghsoob): Usurped property, taken wrongfully.
قيمة (qimah): Market value of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the maximum diyah for a male slave killed accidentally?
A) The full market value.
B) 5,000 dirhams.
C) 10,000 dirhams.
D) 20,000 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The diyah for a slave killed accidentally is capped at 10,000 dirhams (male) and 5,000 (female) in the Hanafi school; full value applies only for usurped slaves.

Diyat — Blood Money

Multiple Assailants and Liability for a Slave's Death
Rulings on multiple assailants beating a slave leading to death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that liability for a slave's death is divided by the number of assailants (not blows) and calculate the arsh shares for each responsible party.

Core Idea
Liability for an injury is apportioned by the number of assailants, not the number of blows—each assailant's share is equal unless some blows are excused.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key Hanafi principle in diyat: when multiple persons strike a slave causing death, liability is divided by the number of assailants, not the number of wounds. Through detailed cases, we see how permitted blows (by a master on his slave or with permission) are disregarded, and how the 'aqilah (agnatic group) bears the blood money for fatal injuries. This principle ensures fairness in shared liability and requires careful accounting of each assailant's role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assailants and classify each blow
List every person who struck the slave. Determine which blows are excused (master on own slave, blows with permission) and which are actionable (unauthorized blows). Only actionable blows count toward the death.
In the first case, the ordered person's first two lashes are excused (permission), his third is actionable; the master's lash is excused; the stranger's is actionable.
2
Apply the principle of counting assailants
The death is divided equally among the assailants who made actionable blows. The master is not an assailant here (his blow excused), so the death is split between the ordered person and the stranger: two-thirds on ordered, one-third on stranger.
The text: 'فانقسم عليهما أثلاثاً؛ ثلث على الأجنبي وثلثاه تلف بجناية المأمور الأول'
3
Calculate arsh for each assailant
The arsh for each actionable blow is the proportion of the slave's value corresponding to that blow's share in the death. The ordered person's arsh is for his third lash (1/6 of value) but reduced by the total number of blows (4). The stranger's arsh is for his single lash (1/3 of value) also reduced.
The exact math: ordered's arsh = (1/6 of value) × (4 lashes total)? Actually the text says 'سدس قيمته مضروباً أربعة أسواط' meaning one-sixth of his value multiplied by four lashes? But it's a fractional calculation. Focus on the principle.
4
Determine who pays: 'aqilah vs. personal wealth
If the injury leads to death, the arsh is on the 'aqilah (blood money group). If the injury is sub-lethal (below the soul), the assailant pays from his own wealth. In the case, the death is fatal, so 'aqilah bears the arsh.
Exception: if the assailant is a master beating his own slave, no payment.
5
Extend to the second scenario (partial ownership and manumission)
When a partner beats a jointly owned slave with a conditional manumission, liability becomes more complex due to ownership fractions and the concept of sirayah (spreading of injury). The rules of mukatab and manumission by guarantee (tamlīk bil-ḍamān) apply.
This part is incomplete in our passage; the full case would demonstrate how shares are adjusted for ownership and manumission.

منقوصاً بأربعة أسواط، وإذا مات العبد من هذه فقد مات من خمس جنايات فانقسم تلف التلف على الجنايات فيقسم عليها لأن العبرة لعدد الجاني لا لعدد الجنايات فانقسم عليهما أثلاثاً؛ ثلث على الأجنبي وثلثاه تلف
