لشريكه إن كان موسراً نصف قيمته مضروباً سوطين وعلى الضارب أرش السوط الثالث مضروباً سوطين ونصف قيمته مضروباً ثلاثة أسواط فيكون ذلك على عاقلته فليستوفها أولياء العبد أو يأخذ المعتق من ذلك ما غرم ويكون الباقي لورثة العبد لأن السوط الأول كله هدر لأن نصفه في ملكه ونصفه لاقى ملك شريكه ولكنه بإذنه، والسوط الثاني نصفه هدر ونصفه معتبر لأن نصفه لا في ملكه ونصفه لا في ملك شريكه بغير إذنه فيضمن أرش السوط الثاني مضروباً سوطاً ماله لشريكه لأن سرايته انقطعت لما أعتقه فاقتصرت الجناية على ما دون النفس فتجب في مال الجاني، وصار العبد كله ملكاً للمعتق بالضمان لأن المعتق بالضمان يملك نصيب الضارب عند أبي حنيفة ويصير مكاتباً له لأنه يوقف عتق هذا النصف على أداء السعاية إليه، فالسوط الثالث لاقى مكاتب غيره فيكون معتبراً كله فيضمن الضارب جميع ما نقصه الوسط الثالث مضروباً سوطين لأن السوط الثالث حل به وهو منقوص سوطين، فلما مات العبد فقد مات من ثلاث جنايات إلا أن الجنايتين الأوليين كجناية واحدة لاتفاق حكمها واتحاده وانهدرت سرايتهما والجناية الثالثة معتبرة بأصلها وسرايتها وإن عتق العبد بعد ذلك لأن إعتاق المكاتب لا يقطع السراية لما بينا فصارت النفس تالفة بجنايتين : إحداهما معتبرة والأخرى مهدرة فيهدر نصف قيمته ويضمن الضارب نصف قيمته مضروباً ثلاثة أسواط لأنه مات منقوصاً ثلاثة أسواط فإن ظفر المعتق بماله كان له أن يأخذ ماله من ما ضمن لشريكه كما له ورثة وللحالف لأن ولاءه له ولم يباشر قتله وإنما أمر بقتله فيكون مسبباً لقتله، والمتسبب للقتل لا يحرم عن الإرث. وإن كان المعتق معسراً فلا ضمان عليه وعلى الضارب الضمان كما وصفنا ويكون نصفه في ماله ونصفه على العاقلة فيأخذ الضارب ذلك نصف قيمة العبد مضروباً سوطين، فإن بقي شيء فلورثة العبد لأن الحالف متى كان معسراً لا يكون للضارب تضمين الحالف وإنما له استسعاء نصيبه فبقي نصيب الضارب على ملكه وصار نصيبه مكاتباً له لأنه توقف عتق نصيبه على أداء السعاية إليه ونصيب المعتق من
He said in al-Jami': The issues of beating are in three chapters: one is the beating of a slave by his master, the second is one of two partners ordering the beating of a jointly owned slave, and the third is the beating by a partner or a stranger. The principle in injuries is to consider the multiplicity of the assailant, not the multiplicity of the injury, because the soul recovers from many wounds and dies from few wounds, so consideration of their length, width, and depth is dropped. A man ordered [another] to beat his slave two lashes, so he beat him three, and the master beat him one lash, then a stranger beat him one lash, then he died from all of that. So on the 'aqilah of the ordered one (by two lashes) is the arsh of the third lash multiplied, which is one-sixth of his value multiplied by four lashes, and on the 'aqilah of the stranger is the arsh of the fifth lash multiplied by four lashes, which is one-third of his value multiplied by four lashes, and what is beyond that is void. Because the ordered one beat him three lashes: two of them are disregarded due to [؟] the inclusion of the permission, and the third is considered because he beat without permission, so he guarantees its arsh including both [؟], the fourth is disregarded because the master's injury to his slave is disregarded, and the fifth is considered, so the stranger guarantees its arsh reduced by four lashes. If the slave dies from these, then he died from five injuries, so the loss is divided over the injuries, because the consideration is for the number of assailants, not the number of injuries. So it was divided between them in thirds: one third on the stranger and two-thirds lost by the injury of the first ordered one, then this two-thirds was divided into halves: half disregarded and half considered. The second principle: that injury to slaves, if it destroys a life or a limb and leads to death, the 'aqilah bears it because it is a blood guarantee, and the blood guarantee is borne by the 'aqilah. If it is limited to below the soul, its guarantee is in the property of the assailant. A slave between two men: one of them said, 'I will beat him one lash; if you increase, then he is free.' So he beat him three, and he died from all of that. So on the beater is half the arsh of two lashes reduced by one lash from his wealth, and on the manumitter for his partner, if he is wealthy, is half his value multiplied by two lashes, and on the beater is the arsh of the third lash multiplied by two lashes and half his value multiplied by three lashes, so that is on his 'aqilah. So let the heirs of the slave collect it, or the manumitter takes from that what he paid, and the rest is for the slave's heirs. Because the first lash is entirely disregarded because half of it was in his ownership and half met his partner's ownership but with his permission. The second lash: half is disregarded and half is considered because half was not in his ownership and half not in his partner's ownership without his permission, so he guarantees the arsh of the second lash multiplied by one lash from his property to his partner because its seriality was cut off when he manumitted, so the injury was limited to below the soul, so it is owed from the assailant's property. And the slave became entirely the property of the manumitter by guarantee, because the manumitter by guarantee owns the share of the beater according to Abu Hanifah, and he becomes his mukatab because he suspends the emancipation of this half on payment of the earning to him. So the third lash met a mukatab of someone else, so it is entirely considered, so the beater guarantees all that the third lash diminished multiplied by two lashes, because the third lash struck him and he was reduced by two lashes. When the slave died, he died from three injuries, except that the first two injuries are like one injury due to the agreement of their ruling and their unity, and their seriality is disregarded, and the third injury is considered in its origin and seriality, even if the slave is emancipated after that because the emancipation of a mukatab does not cut off seriality as we explained. So the soul is destroyed by two injuries: one considered and the other disregarded, so half his value is disregarded and the beater guarantees half his value multiplied by three lashes because he died reduced by three lashes. If the manumitter gets hold of his property, he may take his property from what he guaranteed to his partner, as his heirs and the swearer have, because his wala' is for him and he did not directly kill him, but rather ordered his killing, so he is a cause of his killing, and the cause of killing is not deprived of inheritance. If the manumitter is insolvent, then there is no guarantee upon him, and upon the beater is the guarantee as we described, and half of it is from his wealth and half on the 'aqilah. So the beater takes that: half the value of the slave multiplied by two lashes. If anything remains, it is for the heirs of the slave, because when the swearer is insolvent, the beater does not have the option to make the swearer liable, but only has the option of seeking earnings from his share. So the beater's share remained in his ownership, and his share became a mukatab for him because his emancipation was suspended on payment of the earning to him, and the share of the manumitter from...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, presenting cases from al-Jami' al-Saghir on multiple assailants beating a slave leading to death. The key principle is that liability is apportioned according to the number of assailants, not the number of injuries. The author illustrates with two scenarios: (1) A master orders someone to beat his slave two lashes, but the ordered beats three, the master beats one, and a stranger beats one, resulting in death. The liability is divided: the ordered's extra lash and the stranger's lash are considered, while the permitted lashes and the master's lash are disregarded. The 'aqilah of the ordered and the stranger bear the arsh proportionally. (2) A jointly owned slave: one partner says 'I will beat him one lash; if you increase, he is free.' The other beats three, resulting in death. Complex calculations follow, considering partial ownership, manumission by guarantee, and the status of mukatab. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence. Note that the text is from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, and it is expanding on the matn's principles.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of male agnatic relatives who pay diyah on behalf of a killer.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a body part or for death, synonymous with diyah in some contexts.
سراية (sirayah): The spreading of injury that leads to death, attributing death to the original wound.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract of manumission with his master, paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of the passage, what fraction of the death is attributed to the ordered man (al-ma'mur) after considering the excused blows?
A) One-third (1/3)
B) Two-thirds (2/3) of which half is disregarded, so effective one-third (1/3)
C) All (1/1) because he struck three times
D) One-half (1/2)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when multiple assailants cause a slave's death, liability is divided by the number of assailants with actionable blows, not by the number of injuries; each assailant's share is equal unless some blows are excused by permission or ownership.

Division of Liability for Five Lashes on a Slave
Explains how liability is divided when a slave dies from five lashes from different actors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of proportional liability to a scenario where multiple aggressors inflict injuries on a slave, distinguishing between compensable and non-compensable acts.

Core Idea
Liability for death from multiple injuries is divided by the number of aggressors, not injuries — and permission or master status nullifies compensation.

In this lesson, we examine a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on how to divide liability when a slave dies from five lashes inflicted by different individuals. The central principle is that the number of aggressors determines the shares, not the number of blows. Moreover, certain lashes are considered 'null' (hadar) if they were delivered with permission or by the master, while others are compensable. Understanding this division is crucial for grasping the Hanafi approach to multiple causation in liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and their acts
List the five lashes and who inflicted each: the first commanded one (with permission), the outsider (third lash), the master (fourth lash), and another outsider (fifth lash). Note that the first two lashes are grouped together.
The text assumes a specific scenario; focus on the roles.
2
Classify each lash as mu'tabar or hadar
Determine which lashes give rise to compensation: the third and fifth are mu'tabar (compensable) because inflicted without permission or by an outsider; the first two and fourth are hadar (null) due to permission or master status.
Hadar means the injury is disregarded for liability.
3
Determine the basis for division
The text states 'the consideration is for the number of aggressors, not the number of injuries.' Thus, the total loss is divided into shares based on the number of aggressors (three: the first commanded one, the outsider, and the fifth outsider).
Even though there are five lashes, there are three distinct persons causing injury.
4
Compute the fractions
The loss is divided into thirds: one-third on the outsider (third lash) and two-thirds on the first commanded one (first two lashes). The two-thirds from the first commanded one are then split: half is hadar (due to permission) and half is mu'tabar (because the third lash was without permission). Thus, the first commanded one's net compensable share is one-third (half of two-thirds).
The master's lash (fourth) is entirely hadar, so no share is assigned to him.
5
Identify the underlying principle
The ruling illustrates the Hanafi maxim 'al-ibratu li'adadi al-jānī lā li'adadi al-jināyāt' (consideration is by number of aggressors, not injuries). Also, permission and master status nullify liability. This is a specific application of siraayah (the spreading effect of injuries) in determining proportional liability.
This principle is used when multiple causes contribute to death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في مع السراية للإذن والثالث معتبر لأنه ضرب بغير إذن فيضمن أرشه مضموناً بهما، والرابع هدر لأن جناية المولى على مملوكه هدر، والخامس معتبر فيضمن الأجنبي أرشه منقوصاً بأربعة أسواط، وإذا مات العبد من هذه فقد مات من خمس جنايات فانقسم تلف التلف على الجنايات فيقسم عليها لأن العبرة لعدد الجاني لا لعدد الجنايات فانقسم عليهما أثلاثاً؛ ثلث على الأجنبي وثلثاه تلف بجناية المأمور الأول فانقسم هذا الثلث نصفين؛ نصفه هدر ونصفه معتبر
In the case of continuity due to permission, the third (lash) is considered because he struck without permission, so he guarantees its compensation combined with the two. The fourth is null because the master's injury to his slave is null. The fifth is considered, so the outsider guarantees its compensation reduced by four lashes. If the slave dies from these, he has died from five injuries. The total loss is divided over the injuries, divided over them because consideration is for the number of aggressors, not the number of injuries. So it is divided between them in thirds: one-third on the outsider, and two-thirds are lost due to the injury of the first commanded one. Then this third is divided into two halves: half null and half considered.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining the division of liability when a slave dies from five lashes inflicted by different persons. The first two lashes were given with permission and their effects are nullified. The third lash was without permission, so its compensation is combined with the first two. The fourth lash by the master is null because the master cannot be liable for injuring his own slave. The fifth lash by an outsider is considered, and he pays compensation reduced by four lashes. Upon death, the loss is divided according to the number of aggressors, not injuries. Thus, two-thirds of the liability falls on the first commanded person and one-third on the outsider. The two-thirds from the first commanded one are further split: half is null (due to permission) and half is considered. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle of proportional liability for multiple contributing causes.

Key Terms
سراية (siraayah): The effect of an injury spreading and causing death.
هدر (hadar): A legal term meaning that no compensation is due; the injury is disregarded.
معتبر (mu'tabar): Considered valid; the injury gives rise to compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a slave dies from five lashes, how is the total loss divided between the aggressors?
A) Equally among all five.
B) Two-thirds on the first commanded one and one-third on the outsider.
C) All on the outsider.
D) All on the master.

Key Takeaway
When multiple injuries cause death, Hanafi law divides liability based on the number of aggressors, not injuries, and accounts for permission and master status.

Liability When a Joint Owner Orders Beating and Then Emancipates
Ruling on liability when a joint owner orders a beating with condition and the slave dies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of proportional liability, siraayah, and guarantee to a multi-lash beating scenario involving a jointly owned slave and conditional emancipation.

Core Idea
Each lash is assessed separately based on ownership and permission; liability shifts with emancipation and guarantee.

This lesson examines a dense ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that illustrates how Hanafi fiqh handles a chain of events: one joint owner orders a beating with a condition, the beater exceeds, the slave dies. The ruling meticulously apportions liability for each lash, considering ownership, permission, and the effect of emancipation. You will work through the reasoning step by step, understanding how the school determines who pays what and why.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Identify the key facts: two joint owners, one gives conditional permission (one lash, if exceed then free), the beater gives three lashes, the slave dies from all three. Note the ownership shares (half each) and the permission status.
The condition 'if you increase, he is free' becomes relevant after the second lash, as it triggers a change in the slave's legal status.
2
Analyze each lash individually
For each lash, determine whether the beater had permission and whether the lash fell on the beater's own share, the partner's share, or a combination. The first lash: full permission, half on each share. The second lash: no permission for the partner's share. The third lash: falls after emancipation and guarantee, so the slave's status has changed.
The first lash is entirely null (hader) because it was with permission and on his own property as well.
3
Apply the rule of siraayah (effect of injury)
When the emancipation occurs after the second lash, the siraayah (spreading effect) of the injury is cut off for the first two lashes. The third lash, however, occurs after the emancipation and its siraayah continues to death.
Emancipation of a mukatab does not cut siraayah, but here the emancipation is conditional and the beater's share becomes mukatab.
4
Determine liability for each lash and overall
Calculate the compensation: first lash - no liability; second lash - half of its compensation (from non-owner non-permission part) from the beater's wealth to the partner; third lash - full compensation of its diminution plus half the slave's value (since death from two injuries one null one considered). Also consider whether the emancipator is wealthy or insolvent.
When the beater guarantees compensation for the second lash, he effectively buys the partner's share, making the slave entirely his (or the emancipator's by guarantee) and turning his share into a mukatab.
5
Handle the special cases: wealthy vs insolvent emancipator
If the emancipator is wealthy, he pays half the slave's value to his partner (since he acquires the beater's share by guarantee). If insolvent, the beater bears the full liability, half from his wealth and half on his 'aqlah, and the beater's share remains a mukatab.
The 'aqlah pays for unintentional killing; here the beating is intentional, but the death is considered unintentional regarding the third lash? Actually the ruling places half on 'aqlah for the third lash portion, indicating it is treated like unintentional homicide.

Source Text

عبد بين رجلين قال أحدهما أضربه سوطاً فإن زدت فهو حر فضربه ثلاثة فمات من ذلك كله فعلى الضارب نصف أرش السوطين منقوصاً سوطاً في ماله، وعلى المعتق لشريكه إن كان موسراً نصف قيمته مضروباً سوطين وعلى الضارب A slave owned by two men; one said, 'Beat him one lash; if you increase, he is free.' So he beat him three, and he died from all of that. Then on the beater is half the compensation of the two lashes reduced by one lash from his wealth. And on the one who emancipated to his partner, if he is wealthy, half his value beaten two lashes. And on the beater is the compensation of the third lash beaten two lashes and half his value beaten three lashes; that is on his 'aqlah. The slave's heirs should collect it, or the emancipator may take from that what he paid, and the rest is for the slave's heirs. Because the first lash is entirely null, since half of it was in his ownership and half met the ownership of his partner but with his permission. The second lash: half is null and half considered, because half is not in his ownership and half is not in his partner's ownership without his permission, so he guarantees the compensation of the second lash beaten one lash from his wealth to his partner, because its effect [siraayah] was cut off when he emancipated him, so the injury was confined to what is less than life, so it is due from the wealth of the assailant. And the slave became entirely the property of the emancipator due to guarantee, because the emancipator, by guarantee, owns the share of the beater according to Abu Hanifah, and he becomes his mukatab, because he suspends the emancipation of this half on payment of sa'y to him. So the third lash met the mukatab of another, so it is entirely considered; thus the beater guarantees all the diminution of the third beat beaten two lashes, because the third lash fell on him while he was reduced by two lashes. When the slave died, he died from three injuries, except that the first two injuries are like one injury due to agreement of their ruling and unity, and their effect was nullified, and the third injury is considered in its origin and effect, even if the slave was emancipated after that, because the emancipation of the mukatab does not cut off the effect. So the life was destroyed by two injuries: one considered and one null; so half his value is nullified and the beater guarantees half his value beaten three lashes, because he died reduced by three lashes. If the emancipator acquires his wealth, he may take his wealth from what he guaranteed to his partner, just as he has heirs and for the swearer, because his wala' is for him and he did not directly kill him, but only ordered his killing, so he is a cause of his killing, and the one who causes killing is not deprived of inheritance. If the emancipator is insolvent, then there is no guarantee on him, and on the beater is the guarantee as described; half of it from his wealth and half on the 'aqlah. The beater takes that: half the value of the slave beaten two lashes. If anything remains, it is for the heirs of the slave, because when the swearer is insolent, the beater cannot make the swearer liable, but he has the right to seek payment from his share, so the share of the beater remains in his ownership and his share becomes a mukatab for him, because his emancipation is suspended on payment of sa'y to him, and the share of the emancipator becomes free with his wala'. The first lash was null, the second lash half null and half considered, and the third lash entirely considered, because half is a mukatab for the beater and half for the master of the swearer. The slave died by two injuries: one considered and one null. So on the beater is half the value of the slave beaten three lashes; half of that is on the 'aqlah, because half is a mukatab and half is the emancipated of the swearer. The obligation of his injury on his own mukatab is from his wealth, and the obligation of his injury on the emancipated of another is on his 'aqlah. That becomes the earnings of the mukatab, so the beater collects from it the amount of half his value beaten two lashes, because he takes from his wealth the money of his injury, since it became a debt on him; he also takes from his estate after his death.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a complex case: a slave owned jointly by two persons. One owner says to the other, 'Beat him one lash; if you exceed, he is free.' The other beats him three lashes, and the slave dies. The ruling details the liability of each party. The first lash is entirely null because it was with permission and within ownership. The second lash: half null (permission for one owner's share) and half considered (no permission for the other share). The beater pays compensation for that half to the partner. The third lash is entirely considered because it falls on the slave after the second lash triggered a change in status: the beater's share becomes a mukatab (slave contracted for freedom) due to the guarantee. The liability is apportioned based on the status at the time of each lash. The emancipating owner, if wealthy, must pay half the slave's value to his partner. If insolvent, the beater bears the full liability, half from his wealth and half on his 'aqlah. The ruling also addresses inheritance and collection of compensation. This illustrates the Hanafi principles of proportional liability, the effect of emancipation on ongoing injuries, and the role of the 'aqlah for unintentional killings.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of emancipation and must pay for his freedom.
عاقلة ('aqlah): The group (tribal or professional) that pays blood money on behalf of a killer.
استسعاء (istis'a'): Forcing a slave to earn money to pay for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ruling, why is the third lash entirely considered (mu'tabar) while the first two are partially nullified?
A) Because the third lash was without any permission.
B) Because the third lash fell on a mukatab, not on a fully owned slave.
C) Because the slave died from the third lash alone.
D) Because the beater had full ownership at the time of the third lash.

Key Takeaway
Liability for successive injuries on a jointly owned slave is apportioned per lash based on permission and ownership, with emancipation and guarantee altering the status mid-sequence.

Liability When Multiple Parties Add Lashes Resulting in Death
Ruling on liability when the master and an outsider each add lashes after previous beating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the liability of each participant when multiple lashes are inflicted by different parties, applying the Hanafi rules of diyat and causation.

Core Idea
When multiple lashes—some null, some considered—cause death, liability is apportioned based on the legal status of the victim at each strike.

This lesson extends the previous scenario of a slave being beaten, where the commander (master) and an outsider each add lashes. The ruling demonstrates how the Hanafi school meticulously divides liability considering the changing status of the slave (partially emancipated, then a mukatab) and the relationships of the strikers. You will learn to apply the principles of nullification of some lashes and consideration of others to apportion diyah correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the sequence of lashes
There is a slave previously beaten three lashes. The commander (master) then beats a fourth lash, and an outsider beats a fifth lash. The slave dies from all five lashes.
2
Determine the legal status at each lash
The first lash is null because it was with permission (from previous scenario). The second lash is half considered because it struck partially the partner's share. After the second lash, the swearer emancipated his share, making the slave part free and part mukatab. The third lash strikes a person half free (emancipated) half mukatab. The fourth lash strikes a person half owned by the commander (as master) and half his mukatab. The fifth lash strikes the same person.
The status of the victim at each lash determines whether the lash is 'considered' or 'null' and how much compensation is due.
3
Calculate liability for each lash
The compensation for each considered lash is calculated as a fraction of the slave's value, then combined. The commander pays half the value (because he struck his own mukatab and the fourth lash was fully considered). The outsider's 'aqlah pays one-third. The original beater (the one who gave the first three lashes) pays one-sixth on his 'aqlah. Total fractions add up to one.
These fractions come from the text: 1/6 + 1/2 + 1/3 = 1.
4
Apply inheritance and wala' rules
The collected compensation is distributed: the commanded one takes an amount for the slave (as earnings), what remains goes to the 'asabah of the master commander, but the commander himself is excluded because he killed his mukatab (and thus cannot inherit through wala').
The commander's exclusion is due to the rule that a person who causes death cannot inherit from the victim.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) may hold that the commander is not considered to have killed his mukatab in a way that prohibits inheritance, leading to a different distribution.
This ikhtilaf affects the final shares of the 'asabah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت المسألة بحالها ثم ضربه الأمر وطاً ثم ضربه الأجنبي سوطاً ومات من ذلك كله فعلى المأمور نصف أرش السوط الثاني مضروباً سوطاً في ماله لشريكه وعلى عاقله المأمور إن كان المعتق موسراً أرش السوط الثالث مضروباً سوطين وهو سدس قيمته مضروباً خمسة أسواط في ماله وعلى عاقلة الأجنبي أرش السوط الخامس مضروباً أربعة أسواط وهو ثلث قيمته مضروباً خمسة أسواط لأن السوط الأول كله هدر والسوط الثاني نصفه معتبر لأنه نصفه لاقى ملك شريكه بغير إذنه فيغرم الضارب نصف أرش في ماله لشريكه وسراية الجنايتين مهدرة لأن الحالف أعتق نصيبه بعد السوط الثاني وهو موسر فكان للضارب أن يضمن قيمة نصيبه مضروباً سوطين وصار نصيب الضارب ملكاً للحالف بالضمان وصار مكاتباً له والسوط الثالث معتبر كله لأنه لا في شخصاً نصفه معتق مكاتب له والجناية على المعتق والكاتب معتبرة، والسوط الرابع من المولى لأنه لاقى شخصاً نصفه مولى للآمر ونصفه مكاتب له وجناية الإنسان على مولاه ومكاتبه معتبرة فيغرم الآمر ما نقصه السوط الرابع منقوصاً ثلاثة أسواط، والسوط الخامس من الأجنبي معتبر فيغرم أرش ما نقصه مضروباً أربعة أسواط ، وإذا مات العبد من ذلك كله يغرم الضارب سدس قيمته مضروباً خمسة أسواط لأنه قتل النفس ثلاثة فقد تلفت النفس بجنايات الضارب وهو ثلاثة أسواط إلا أن السوطين الأولين حكمهما واحد فإن سرايتهما مهدرة فتجعل جناية واحدة، والسوط الثالث بأصله وسرايته معتبرة فهذا الثلث تلف بجنايتين إحداهما معتبرة والأخرى مهدرة فيغرم نصف الثلث وذلك سدس الكل ويجب على عاقلته لأنه جنى على معتق ومكاتب غيره ويضمن الآمر نصف قيمته مضروباً خمسة أسواط في ماله لأنه جنى على المكاتب نفسه لأنه لم يظهر لعتق نصيبه أثر في حكم من أحكام الحرية فكان الكل مكاتباً له حكماً واعتباراً على عاقلة الأجنبي ثلث قيمته مضروباً خمسة أسواط لأنه جنى على مكاتب غيره ومولى غيره يكون من عاقلة الأجنبي ومن الآمر ومن المأمور للعبد لأنه كسب العبد ويأخذ المأمور من الأمر بذلك من مال العبد لأن هذا أرش له على العبد، وما بقي في ماله فلعصبة المولى الآمر إن لم يكن للعبد عصبة لأن الولاء لهما إلا أن الأمر باشر قتله بغير حق محرم عن الميراث فيجعل كالميت فيكون ما بقي لأقرب عصيان الأمر. وقال في

The commander guarantees half his value beaten five lashes from his wealth, because he injured his own mukatab, since there appeared no effect of the emancipation of his share in any ruling of freedom, so the whole was his mukatab in ruling and consideration. The commanded one takes from the slave, because this is compensation to him against the slave. What remains in his wealth is for the 'asabah of the master commander, if the slave has no 'asabah, because the wala' is for both, except that the commander directly killed him without right, which prohibits inheritance, so he is considered dead, so what remains goes to the nearest 'asabah of the commander. Commentary
This passage extends the previous scenario: after the initial three lashes, the master (commander) beats the slave a fourth lash, and an outsider beats a fifth lash, resulting in death. The ruling breaks down liability for each participant. The first two lashes' effects are nullified due to permission and partial ownership. The third lash is considered entirely. The fourth lash by the master is considered because it strikes a person who is part mukatab for the commander. The fifth lash by the outsider is also considered. Upon death, the total liability is divided: the original beater pays one-sixth of the slave's value on his 'aqlah; the commander pays half the value from his wealth; the outsider's 'aqlah pays one-third. The distribution of the collected amounts among the heirs and the 'asabah is also detailed. The passage ends with a reference to another opinion, but the text is cut off. This case illustrates the Hanafi methodology for handling multiple contributing causes with varying legal statuses.

Key Terms
عصبة ('asabah): Male agnatic relatives who inherit by virtue of being blood kin.
ولاء (wala'): The bond of clientage between a manumitter and a freed slave, giving inheritance rights.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, paying installments.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury, less than full diyah.

Diyat — Blood Money

Apportioning Diyat for Multiple Blows on a Partially Free Slave
Detailed calculation of diyat for multiple stakeholders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate diyat liability in a multi-party tort case involving a partially free slave, identifying which blows are considered (mu'tabar) and which are wasted (hadr), and apportioning payments between the striker's own wealth, his 'aqila, and other parties.

Core Idea
Liability for each blow depends on the slave's status (free/slave/mukatab) at the time of the blow and the striker's relationship to the slave (owner/mukatib/stranger).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed case of a slave who is both a mukatab and partially freed, receiving multiple lashes from different persons. The author demonstrates how Hanafi fiqh apportions diyat by considering the slave's evolving legal status and the specific circumstances of each blow. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to handle complex multi-party torts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slave's status at each blow
Determine whether the slave is free, slave, or mukatab at the moment of each lash. This status affects liability: a blow by the owner on his own slave is wasted; a blow by a stranger on a free person is considered.
The slave's status can change between blows, e.g., after manumission or becoming a mukatab.
2
Classify each blow as considered or wasted
Apply the rule: a blow that meets the striker's own property (his slave) is wasted; a blow that meets another's property or a free person is considered. Also, blows that cause death may have spreading (saraya) classified.
In the passage, the first lash is entirely wasted because it was from the master on his own slave.
3
Calculate the diyat for each considered blow
Use the fractions of the slave's value and the number of lashes as given. For example, if a blow reduces the slave's value by a certain fraction, that fraction of the full diyat is due.
The calculations involve fractions like half, third, sixth multiplied by the number of lashes.
4
Determine who bears the liability
Distinguish between the striker's personal wealth and his 'aqila. If the victim is the striker's own mukatab, payment is from his own wealth; if the victim is a freed person of another, payment is on the 'aqila.
The 'aqila is responsible for diyat on behalf of a free perpetrator when the victim is a free person.
5
Account for the slave's death and distribution of estate
When the slave dies, compute the total liability from all considered blows. The slave's estate is distributed to claimants, including the striker who may take from it if he has a debt.
The passage also addresses how manumission after injury affects the distribution.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed case analysis of diyat when a slave receives multiple lashes from different parties while in a status of partial freedom (mukatab and partially freed). The author works through the legal consequences of each lash, considering the slave's changing status as a mukatab and a freed person. The key principle is that liability is apportioned according to the slave's share of freedom at the time of each lash and the relationship of the striker to the slave (whether owner, mukatib, or stranger). The 'aqila (tribe) is responsible for diyat when the striker is a free person injuring a freed slave of another. The calculation involves fractions of the slave's value and the number of lashes, with careful consideration of which injuries are 'considered' (mu'tabar) and which are 'wasted' (hadr). The passage also addresses the effect of manumission after the injury and the distribution of the slave's estate. This is a classic example of how Hanafi fiqh handles complex multi-party torts.

Key Terms
عاقلة (‘āqila): The tribe or group responsible for paying diyat on behalf of a free perpetrator.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying a sum.
هدر (hadar): Legally void or without effect; here meaning that no diyat is due for that injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of the passage, why is half of the second lash considered and half wasted?
A) Because the slave was half free at that time.
B) Because half of it struck the partner's share without permission.
C) Because the striker was the master of half the slave.
D) Because the 'aqila only pays for half.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for diyat in multi-party torts is apportioned by analyzing the victim's status at each injury and the legal relationship between striker and victim.

Critique of Diyat Estimation for Slave's Limbs vs. Life
Critique of a Hanafi ruling on estimating diyat for a slave's limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the logical discrepancy in the Hanafi discussion on diyat for a slave's limbs versus his life.

Core Idea
If a slave's limb diyat exceeds his life diyat, the ruling becomes absurd – estimation must be consistent.

This passage from al-Nihayah critiques a specific view on how to calculate diyat for a slave's limbs. The critique highlights that a simple proportional estimation can lead to the illogical result that compensation for a limb may be greater than for taking the entire life. We will unpack the reasoning and explore the Hanafi responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criticized view
Locate the statement in the passage that describes the estimation method: limbs are guaranteed by calculation proportionally to the slave's value, and the slave is handed over for cutting limbs.
The view treats limbs as property, similar to damage to wealth.
2
Understand the estimation method
For a slave valued at 100,000 dirhams, cutting a hand would require 50,000 dirhams (half), while killing him would require 10,000 dirhams minus ten (the diyat for his life).
The exact figures illustrate the disproportionate result.
3
Trace the logical outcome
Follow the arithmetic: if the value is high, the limb diyat can exceed the life diyat. This is inconsistent because the loss of a limb is less severe than loss of life.
4
Recognize the absurdity
The author calls this 'odious' (شنيع) – it violates the principle that compensation should reflect the gravity of harm.
Such a ruling would be unacceptable in practice.
5
Explore proposed resolutions
The passage offers two answers: either the estimation applies only when the injury spreads to life or causes loss of benefit type, or one uses calculation without reduction – but the latter leads to absurdity, so it is rejected.
The critique points to a flaw in the reasoning, not a settled fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في النهاية : هذا بخلاف ظاهر الرواية لأنه ذكر في المبسوط ففي طرف المملوك تعتبر بأطراف الحر من الدية إلى آخره. فإن قيل : عند الإمام يدفع إليه العبد ويأخذ قيمته في قطع الأطراف فأي تقدير على قوله؟ فالجواب أن التقدير على قوله فيما إذا جنى عليه آخر بقطع يد أو رجل فسرى فيه إلى النفس أو فوت جنس المنفعة في عدم التقدير والدفع في غيره، وقيل يضمن في الأطراف بحسابه بالغة ما بلغت ولا ينقص منه شيء لأن الأطراف يسلك فيها مسلك الأموال، وهذا يؤدي إلى أمر شنيع وهو أن ما يجب في الأطراف أكثر مما يجب في النفوس بأن كانت قيمته مثلا مائة ألف فإنه بقطع يده يجب خمسون ألفاً ويقتله يجب عشرة آلاف إلا عشرة.
He said in al-Nihayah: This contradicts the zahir al-riwayah because it is mentioned in al-Mabsut that for a limb of a slave, it is considered like the limbs of a free person from the diya etc. If it is said: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave is handed over and his value is taken in cutting of limbs, so what estimation is upon his statement? The answer: The estimation upon his statement is in the case where another injured him by cutting a hand or leg that spread to the soul or caused loss of the type of benefit in the absence of estimation and payment in other than that. And it is said: He guarantees in limbs by his calculation reaching whatever amount and nothing is decreased from it because limbs are treated in the manner of wealth. This leads to an odious thing: that what is obligatory in limbs is more than what is obligatory in souls; for example, if his value is one hundred thousand, then for cutting his hand fifty thousand becomes obligatory, and for killing him ten thousand except ten becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the work al-Nihayah, pointing out a contradiction with the zahir al-riwayah (the well-known transmitted rulings). The issue is the method of estimating diyat for a slave's limbs. According to one view (attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa), the slave is handed over and his value is taken. But this leads to a problem: if the slave's value is high, the diyat for a limb (e.g., half the value) may exceed the diyat for killing him (which is the slave's value, but with a reduction? Actually the text says 'ten thousand except ten' which seems a miscalculation or scribal error). The text provides two possible answers: either the estimation applies only to cases where the injury spreads to life or causes loss of type of benefit, or one guarantees by calculation without reduction, but this leads to the absurdity that limb diyat could exceed life diyat. The passage thus highlights a logical problem in the ruling.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāya): The well-known and authoritative transmissions within the Hanafi school, primarily from Imam Muhammad's six books.
تقدير (taqdīr): Estimation or valuation; here referring to how diyat for a slave's limb is calculated based on his value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'odious thing' mentioned in the passage?
A) That giving blood money is always odious.
B) That the diyat for a limb can exceed the diyat for killing the slave.
C) That the slave's value is taken instead of diyat.
D) That the 'aqila is not responsible.

Key Takeaway
The diyat for a slave's limbs must be estimated in a way that does not exceed the diyat for his life, otherwise the ruling becomes internally inconsistent.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Qisas for a Wounded Slave Freed Then Dies
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for a freed slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad al-Shaybani on this issue, and explain the key arguments regarding the basis of guardianship and the effect of manumission on saraya.

Core Idea
When a wounded slave is freed and dies, the right to qisas hinges on whether manumission cuts off the causal link and whether the basis of guardianship matters.

This passage examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding qisas for a slave who was wounded by a third party, then freed by his master, and subsequently died from the wound. The central issue is whether the master, who was the owner at the time of injury and later becomes the sole heir, can demand qisas. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that certainty of the master's right allows qisas, while Muhammad al-Shaybani contends that the difference in the basis of guardianship (ownership vs. wala') creates ambiguity, and that manumission interrupts the spread (saraya) of the injury, precluding qisas. The passage illustrates how analogical reasoning and consideration of legal cause (illah) lead to divergent rulings within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and subcases
The passage presents a scenario: a man cuts off a slave's hand, the master frees the slave, then the slave dies from the wound. Two subcases are distinguished: (1) the slave has heirs other than the master, and (2) the master is the sole heir. The first subcase is agreed upon (no qisas), and the dispute concerns the second.
The matn (text) states the ruling for both subcases, then the commentary explains the reasoning.
2
Understand the ambiguity in the first subcase
When there are other heirs, the right to qisas is ambiguous because the master's claim stems from ownership at the time of injury, while the heirs' claim stems from inheritance at death. Since these rights cannot both be exercised permanently, qisas is not enforced.
This part is not disputed; it serves as a backdrop for the second subcase.
3
Analyze the dispute in the second subcase (sole heir)
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that since the master is the only heir, there is no ambiguity: the right to qisas is certain. Muhammad counters that the basis of guardianship differs (ownership vs. wala'), creating legal uncertainty, and also that manumission cuts off saraya (the spread of injury), so the wound and death are treated as independent events.
Muhammad uses analogies: the case of a disputed slave-girl (claiming purchase vs. marriage) and distinguishes a debt acknowledgment case.
4
Evaluate the analogical arguments
Muhammad draws an analogy to a slave-girl dispute where one claims purchase and another claims marriage; intercourse is prohibited due to uncertainty. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond by analogizing to a debt acknowledgment case where difference of basis does not matter because the ruling is unified. The passage shows how different analogies lead to different results.
The argument also involves the concept of saraya: manumission is considered a 'cut' that severs the causal chain, a uniquely Hanafi point.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Practically, if a similar case arises in a Hanafi court, the judge following Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf would allow qisas when the master is the sole heir, while a judge following Muhammad would not. The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but the matn implicitly adopts Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view.
Understanding this dispute helps in recognizing the range of valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (قطع يد عبد فحرره سيده فمات منه وله ورثة غيره لا يقتص وإلا اقتص منه وإنما لا يقتص في الأول لاشتباه من له الحق لأن القصاص يجب عند الموت مستند إلى وقت الجرح فعلى اعتبار حالة الجرح يكون الحق للمولى وعلى اعتبار الحالة الثانية يكون للورثة فتحقق الاشتباه فتعذر فلا تجب على وجه يستوفي إذ الكلام فيما إذا كان للعبد ورثة أخرى سوى المولى واجتماعهما لا يزيل الاشتباه لأن الملك يثبت لكل واحد منهما في إحدى الحالتين، ولا يثبت على الدوام فيها فلا يكون الاجتماع مقيداً. ولا يقال بأذن كل واحد منهما لصاحبه لأن الإذن إنما يصح إذا كان الآذن يملك ذلك بخلاف العبد الموصى برقبته لرجل وبخدمته لآخر وكل واحد منهما دائم فصار بمنزلة الشريكين فيه فلا يفرد أحدهما دون الآخر لما فيه من إبطال حق الآخر فيتصل باجتماعهما للرضا ببطلان حقه. وأما في الثاني وهو ما إذا لم يكن له ورثة غير المولى فهو قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد رحمه الله : لا يجب القصاص فيه أيضاً لأن سبب الولاية قد اختلف لأن الملك على اعتبار العتق والولاء على اعتبار حالة الموت فنزل اختلاف السبب منزلة اختلاف المستحق فيما لا يثبت مع الشبهة أو فيما يحتاط فيه فصار كما إذا قال لآخر بعتني هذه الجارية وقال لا بل زوجتها منك لا يحل له وطؤها لما قلنا بخلاف ما إذا أقر لرجل بألف درهم من القرض وقال المقر له من ثمن مبيع فإنه يقضى له عليه بألف وإن اختلف السبب لأن الأموال تثبت بالشبهة فلا يبالي باختلاف السبب عند اتحاد الحكم، ولأن الإعتاق قاطع للسراية وبانقطاعها يبقى الجرح بلا سراية والسراية بلا قطع فيمتنع القصاص ولهما أنهما تيقنا ثبوت الولاية للمولى فيستوفيه، وهذا لأن المقضى له معلوم والحكم متحد فأمكن الإيجاب والاستيفاء لاتحاد المستوفي والمستوفى منه ولا معتبر باختلاف السبب بعد ذلك كمسألة الإقرار بخلاف الفصل الأول لأن المقضى له مجهول، وبخلاف مسألة الجارية لأن الحكم مختلف لأن ملك اليمين يغاير ملك النكاح في الحكم لأن النكاح يثبت الحل مقصوداً وملك اليمين لا يثبته مقصوداً
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (A man cut off the hand of a slave, then his master freed him, and he died from it. If he has heirs other than the master, qisas is not required; otherwise, qisas is required from him. And qisas is not required in the first case due to ambiguity about who has the right because qisas becomes obligatory at death based on the time of the wound. On consideration of the state of the wound, the right belongs to the master; on consideration of the second state (at death), it belongs to the heirs. So ambiguity is realized, thus it is not possible, so it is not obligatory in a manner to be exacted. This discussion is in the case where the slave has other heirs besides the master. Their conjunction does not remove the ambiguity because ownership is established for each of them in one of the two states, and it is not established permanently in them, so the conjunction is not restrictive. And it cannot be said that each one gives permission to the other because permission is only valid if the one giving permission owns that. This is unlike the slave whose person is bequeathed to one man and his service to another, and each is permanent, so he becomes like a partner between them; one cannot be singled out without the other due to the nullification of the other's right, so their conjunction is connected to satisfaction with the nullification of his right. As for the second case, i.e., when he has no heirs other than the master, it is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Qisas is not required in this case either, because the basis of guardianship differed: ownership is based on manumission, while inheritance (wala') is based on the state of death. So the difference of basis is treated as the difference of the entitled person in matters that are not established with doubt or in matters where caution is observed. Thus it becomes like the case where one says to another, 'You sold me this slave-girl,' and he says, 'No, I married her to you'; he cannot have intercourse with her due to what we said. This is unlike the case where one acknowledges a debt of a thousand dirhams to a man as a loan, and the acknowledged says it is from the price of a sale; then the thousand is decreed for him despite the difference in basis because wealth is established with doubt, so difference of basis is not considered when the ruling is unified. And because manumission cuts off the spread (of injury), and with its cessation, the wound remains without spread and the spread remains without a cut, so qisas is prevented. Their (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's) argument is that they are certain of the establishment of guardianship for the master, so he exacts it. This is because the person who is decreed for is known, and the ruling is unified, so it is possible to impose and exact due to the unity of the executor and the one from whom it is exacted. Difference of basis is not considered after that, as in the case of acknowledgement. Unlike the first case because the person decreed for is unknown. And unlike the case of the slave-girl because the ruling differs: ownership of the right hand (milk al-yamin) differs from ownership of marriage in ruling because marriage establishes permissibility as a primary intention, while milk al-yamin does not establish it as a primary intention.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of qisas (retaliation) when a slave has his hand cut off, is then freed by his master, and subsequently dies from the wound. The matn states: if the slave has heirs other than the master, qisas is not required; otherwise, qisas is required. The commentary explains the reasoning behind the first part: there is ambiguity because the right to qisas belongs to the master based on the time of the wound (ownership), but to the heirs based on the time of death (inheritance). This ambiguity prevents qisas. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if there are no other heirs (only the master), qisas is required because the master is certain. Muhammad argues that even then qisas is not required because the basis of guardianship differs (ownership vs. inheritance), and this difference is treated as a difference in the entitled person in matters where caution is required. He draws an analogy with a case of a slave-girl where one claims purchase and the other marriage - intercourse is prohibited. He distinguishes the case from a simple debt acknowledgment where difference of basis does not matter because wealth is established with doubt. A second argument by Muhammad is that manumission cuts off the spread (saraya) of the injury, so the wound becomes independent, making qisas inapplicable. The passage thus shows different approaches to legal reasoning within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind for intentional killing or wounding.
سراية (sarāya): The spreading of an injury that leads to death; if the injury spreads and causes death, it is considered a single act.
ولاء (walā’): The bond of clientage between a freed slave and his former master, giving the master inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, why is qisas not required even when the master is the sole heir?
A) Because the master is not a blood relative.
B) Because the basis of guardianship differs (ownership vs. wala'), creating ambiguity.
C) Because the slave was not fully free at the time of injury.
D) Because the injury did not spread.

Key Takeaway
An intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for a freed slave illustrates how differing views on legal certainty, the effect of manumission on causal chains, and analogical reasoning can lead to divergent rulings within the same school.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Qisas When a Freed Slave Dies from Pre-Freeing Injury
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sirayah and qisas for a freed slave's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding qisas when a slave is injured, freed, then dies, and apply the ruling to determine whether qisas or compensation is due.

Core Idea
When a freed slave dies from a pre-freeing injury, qisas is required only if the master is the sole heir; otherwise, compensation is due.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq dissects a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: does emancipation sever the sirayah (spread) of an injury, thus preventing qisas? The ruling hinges on whether the slave has heirs other than the master. We will walk through the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani to understand the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
A slave's hand is cut by a third party. The master then frees the slave. The slave dies from the injury. Determine if the slave had heirs other than the master.
The case distinguishes between two sub-scenarios: presence or absence of other heirs.
2
State the two scenarios and initial ruling
If the slave has other heirs, no qisas due to confusion over who holds the right of retaliation. If no other heirs, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold qisas is obligatory; Muhammad disagrees.
The confusion arises because the right traces back to the time of injury (master as owner) but at death after freedom, the right would belong to heirs.
3
Extract the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They argue certainty of the master's right. Since the master is the sole heir and the basis of guardianship is certain, qisas is executed. They analogize to acknowledgment of debt despite differing causes.
The judgment is unified, so difference in basis is irrelevant.
4
Extract the opposing position of Imam Muhammad
Muhammad argues that emancipation changes the basis from ownership to wala', creating uncertainty similar to a conflicting claim of sale vs. marriage. He also holds that emancipation cuts off sirayah.
For Muhammad, the difference in basis takes the place of difference in entitled person.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response and determine the mufta bihi
The text favors the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as the master's right is certain and there is no actual confusion. The mufta bihi position is that qisas applies when no other heirs exist.
This is the relied-upon position in the school (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سیده فمات منه وله ورثة غيره لا يقتص وإلا اقتص منه قال أحدكما حر فشجا فبين النهاية : هذا بخلاف ظاهر الرواية لأنه ذكر في المبسوط ففي طرف المملوك تعتبر بأطراف الحر من الدية إلى آخره. فإن قيل : عند الإمام يدفع إليه العبد ويأخذ قيمته في قطع الأطراف فأي تقدير على قوله؟ فالجواب أن التقدير على قوله فيما إذا جنى عليه آخر بقطع يد أو رجل فسرى فيه إلى النفس أو فوت جنس المنفعة في عدم التقدير والدفع في غيره، وقيل يضمن في الأطراف بحسابه بالغة ما بلغت ولا ينقص منه شيء لأن الأطراف يسلك فيها مسلك الأموال، وهذا يؤدي إلى أمر شنيع وهو أن ما يجب في الأطراف أكثر مما يجب في النفوس بأن كانت قيمته مثلا مائة ألف فإنه بقطع يده يجب خمسون ألفاً ويقتله يجب عشرة آلاف إلا عشرة . قال رحمه الله : (قطع يد عبد فحرره سيده فمات منه وله ورثة غيره لا يقتص وإلا اقتص منه وإنما لا يقتص في الأول لاشتباه من له الحق لأن القصاص يجب عند الموت مستند إلى وقت الجرح فعلى اعتبار حالة الجرح يكون الحق للمولى وعلى اعتبار الحالة الثانية يكون للورثة فتحقق الاشتباه فتعذر فلا تجب على وجه يستوفي إذ الكلام فيما إذا كان للعبد ورثة أخرى سوى المولى واجتماعهما لا يزيل الاشتباه لأن الملك يثبت لكل واحد منهما في إحدى الحالتين، ولا يثبت على الدوام فيها فلا يكون الاجتماع مقيداً. ولا يقال بأذن كل واحد منهما لصاحبه لأن الإذن إنما يصح إذا كان الآذن يملك ذلك بخلاف العبد الموصى برقبته لرجل وبخدمته لآخر وكل واحد منهما دائم فصار بمنزلة الشريكين فيه فلا يفرد أحدهما دون الآخر لما فيه من إبطال حق الآخر فيتصل باجتماعهما للرضا ببطلان حقه. وأما في الثاني وهو ما إذا لم يكن له ورثة غير المولى فهو قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد رحمه الله : لا يجب القصاص فيه أيضاً لأن سبب الولاية قد اختلف لأن الملك على اعتبار العتق والولاء على اعتبار حالة الموت فنزل اختلاف السبب منزلة اختلاف المستحق فيما لا يثبت مع الشبهة أو فيما يحتاط فيه فصار كما إذا قال لآخر بعتني هذه الجارية وقال لا بل زوجتها منك لا يحل له وطؤها لما قلنا بخلاف ما إذا أقر لرجل بألف درهم من القرض وقال المقر له من ثمن مبيع فإنه يقضى له عليه بألف وإن اختلف السبب لأن الأموال تثبت بالشبهة فلا يبالي باختلاف السبب عند اتحاد الحكم، ولأن الإعتاق قاطع للسراية وبانقطاعها يبقى الجرح بلا سراية والسراية بلا قطع فيمتنع القصاص ولهما أنهما تيقنا ثبوت الولاية للمولى فيستوفيه، وهذا لأن المقضى له معلوم والحكم متحد فأمكن الإيجاب والاستيفاء لاتحاد المستوفي والمستوفى منه ولا معتبر باختلاف السبب بعد ذلك كمسألة الإقرار بخلاف الفصل الأول لأن المقضى له مجهول، وبخلاف مسألة الجارية لأن الحكم مختلف لأن ملك اليمين يغاير ملك النكاح في الحكم لأن النكاح يثبت الحل مقصوداً وملك اليمين لا يثبته مقصوداً وقد لا يثبت الحل أصلاً، ولأن ما ادعى كل واحد منهما من السبب للحل انتفى بانكار الآخر فبقي بلا سبب فلا يثبت الحل بدونه إذا لا يجري فيه البدل بخلاف ما نحن فيه لأن السبب موجود بيقين ولا منكر له فلم يوجد ما يبطله ولا ما يحتمل الإبطال فأمكن استيفاؤه، والإعتاق لا يقطع السراية لذاته بل الاشتباه من له الحق وذلك إذا كان له وارث آخر غير المولى على ما بينا أو في الأطراف أو في القتل خطأ لأن العبد لا يصلح مالكاً للمال فعلى اعتبار حالة الجرح يكون الحق للمولى وعلى اعتبار حالة الموت أو زيادة الجرح في الحالة الثانية يكون للعبد حتى تقضى منه ديونه وتنفذ وصاياه فحصل الاشتباه فيمن له الحق فسقط . ما حدث بعد الحرية من ذلك الجرح. وأما القتل عمداً فموجبه القصاص فلا اشتباه فيه إذا لم يكن له وارث سوى المولى لأنه على اعتبار أن يكون الحق للعبد فالمولى هو الذي يتولاه فلا اشتباه فيمن له الحق ، فالحاصل من هذا كله أن من قطع يد عبد غيره فأعتقه المولى ثم مات لا يزيد على أربع لأنه إما إن قطع عمداً أو خطأ، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون للعبد وارث سوى المولى أو لم يكن فإن كان يقطع الإعتاق السراية بالاتفاق فلا يجب القصاص لجهالة المقضى له والمقضى به، وإن لم يكن لا يقطعها عندهما خلافاً لمحمد وإن كان الثاني فالإعتاق لا يقطعها فحاصله أنهم أجمعوا في الخطأ وفي العمد فيما إذا كان له وارث آخر أن الإعتاق يقطع السراية فلا يجب إلا أرش القطع وما ينقص بذلك إلا الإعتاق ويسقط الدية والقصاص، وكذا في القطع إذا لم يمت منه لا يجب عليه سوى أرش القطع وما نقص إلى الإعتاق ولا يجب عليه ما حدث من النقصان بعد الإعتاق بالإجماع فعلم بذلك أن كل موضع لا يجب فيه القصاص يجب فيه أرش القطع، وما نقصه إلى الإعتاق ولا يجب عليه الدية وما نقص منه بعد الإعتاق .
If the master frees him (the slave) and he dies from it and he has heirs other than the master, retaliation is not required; otherwise, retaliation is required from him. The author said: (He cut the hand of a slave, then his master freed him, and he died from it, and he has heirs other than the master, then no retaliation; otherwise, retaliation is taken from him). The reason no retaliation in the first case is due to confusion as to who has the right, because retaliation becomes obligatory at the time of death but is traced back to the time of injury. Considering the time of injury, the right belongs to the master; considering the later state, it belongs to the heirs. So confusion is established, making it impossible to impose it in a way that can be executed. This is when the slave has other heirs besides the master. The fact that both (master and heirs) are present does not remove the confusion because ownership is established for each of them in one of the two states, not permanently, so their coming together does not restrict it. It cannot be said that each gives permission to the other, because permission is only valid if the one giving permission owns that right. This is unlike the case of a slave whose person is bequeathed to one person and his service to another, where each has a permanent right, so they are like partners; one cannot be singled out without the other because that would invalidate the other's right, and their coming together implies acceptance of the nullification of the right. As for the second case, where there is no heir other than the master, this is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Retaliation is not obligatory here either, because the basis of guardianship differs: ownership is based on emancipation, and inheritance is based on the state at death. The difference in basis takes the place of difference in the entitled person in matters that are not established with doubt or where caution is required. It is like if someone says to another: 'You sold me this slave-girl,' and the other says: 'No, I married her to you,' sexual relations are not permissible. This is different from the case where one claims a debt of a thousand dirhams as a loan and the other says it is from the price of a sold item, where the debtor is ordered to pay the thousand despite the differing basis, because property rights can be established despite doubt when the judgment is unified. Also, emancipation cuts off the spread (of the injury), and with its cessation, the injury remains without spread, and the spread without the injury is impossible, so retaliation is prevented. The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue: They are certain that the master's guardianship is established, so he can execute it. This is because the party entitled is known and the judgment is unified, so both obligation and execution are possible since the executor and the one executed upon are the same. The difference in basis is irrelevant after that, as in the case of the acknowledgment. This is different from the first case because the entitled party is unknown, and different from the slave-girl case because the judgment differs: ownership by right hand differs from marriage in effect, because marriage establishes permissibility as the primary purpose, while ownership by right hand does not establish it primarily, and may not establish it at all. Also, what each claims as the basis for permissibility is negated by the other's denial, so it remains without basis, and permissibility cannot be established without it, since no substitute applies. In our case, the basis exists with certainty and no one denies it, so nothing invalidates it or even potentially invalidates it, so execution is possible. Emancipation does not cut off the spread by itself, but rather the confusion as to who has the right. That confusion occurs when there is another heir besides the master, as explained, or in cases of limbs, or in accidental killing, because the slave cannot own property. Considering the time of injury, the right belongs to the master; considering the time of death or the increase of injury in the second state, it belongs to the slave so that his debts can be paid and his bequests executed. So confusion arises as to who has the right, and thus the right to what happens after freedom from that injury falls away. As for intentional killing, its consequence is retaliation, so there is no confusion when there is no heir other than the master, because even if the right is considered to belong to the slave, the master is the one who executes it, so there is no confusion as to who has the right. The conclusion from all this: If someone cuts off the hand of another's slave, then the master frees him, and then he dies, it does not exceed four possibilities: either it was intentional or accidental. If intentional: either the slave has an heir other than the master or not. If he has another heir, there is consensus that emancipation cuts off the spread, so no retaliation is required due to ignorance of the entitled party and the entitled from. If he has no other heir, then according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it does not cut off, contrary to Muhammad. If it was accidental, then emancipation does not cut it off. The summary: They all agree that in accidental cases, and in intentional cases when there is another heir, emancipation cuts off the spread, so only the compensation for the cut (arsh) is due, and what decreases due to emancipation; the full blood money (diyah) and retaliation are dropped. Similarly, if the cut does not lead to death, only compensation for the cut and the decrease up to emancipation are due, and nothing for the decrease after emancipation by consensus. Thus it is known that wherever retaliation is not required, compensation for the cut and the decrease up to emancipation are due, but not the full blood money or the decrease after emancipation.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a case where someone cuts off the hand of a slave, then the master frees the slave, who subsequently dies from the injury. The ruling depends on whether the slave has heirs other than the master. If he has other heirs, retaliation (qisas) is not required due to uncertainty about who owns the right of retaliation. At the time of the injury, the right belongs to the master (as owner), but at the time of death after emancipation, the right would belong to the heirs. Since both claim the right, confusion arises and qisas cannot be implemented. If the slave has no other heir, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that qisas is required because the master is certain to have the right. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that emancipation changes the basis of guardianship (from ownership to wala'), creating uncertainty similar to a conflicting claim of sale vs. marriage, where the basis differs. The text also clarifies that emancipation cuts off the spread (sirayah) of the injury in cases where there is another heir (by consensus) or in accidental killing. In intentional killing with no other heir, the two imams hold that emancipation does not cut off spread, so qisas stands. The final ruling distinguishes between cases where retaliation is possible and where only compensation (arsh) for the cut and the decrease in value due to emancipation is due.

Key Terms
سراية (sirayah): The spread of the injury from the wound to the death or additional harm.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation for intentional killing or wounding, equivalent to the act.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a non-lethal injury to a slave, calculated as the decrease in value due to the injury and subsequent emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if someone cuts off a slave's hand, the master frees the slave, and the slave dies with no other heirs, what is the ruling?
A) Qisas is required because the master is the sole heir.
B) Only compensation (arsh) for the cut is required because emancipation severs the spread.
C) No liability because the slave became free.
D) Full diyah is required but not qisas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a freed slave dies from a pre-freeing injury, qisas applies only if the master is the sole heir; otherwise, only compensation for the injury and decrease in value is due.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Ambiguous Freeing and Compensation for Wounds
Ruling on compensation when a master ambiguously frees a slave then a wound occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation when an ambiguous declaration of freedom is followed by a wound, and explain why the compensation goes to the master.

Core Idea
An ambiguous freeing does not make a slave free at the moment of injury; compensation for the wound goes to the master.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in diyat (compensation for injuries) involving ambiguous declarations of freedom. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that when a master says 'one of you is free' and then a third party wounds one, the clarification after the wound does not retroactively change the slave's status. The key lies in the Hanafi understanding of bayan (clarification) as partly enactment and partly disclosure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ambiguous Declaration
The master says to his two slaves: 'One of you is free' without specifying which. This is an ambiguous (mubham) statement.
The identity of the freed slave is unknown at this point.
2
Note the Wound and Clarification Timeline
A third party wounds one slave. Later, the master clarifies that the wounded slave was the one he intended to free.
The clarification occurs after the wound.
3
Apply the Ruling: Compensation Belongs to the Master
At the moment of the wound, both slaves were still owned by the master because the freeing had not yet been assigned to a specific person. Therefore, the arsh (compensation for the wound) is paid to the master.
The master is the owner of the slaves at the time of injury.
4
Distinguish from the Death Case
If both slaves are killed simultaneously by a single person, the killer owes the full diyah of a free person (for the one later clarified as free) and the value of a slave (for the other), divided between the master and the free person's heirs.
The difference is that after death, the subject no longer exists for clarification as enactment; only disclosure remains.
5
Understand the Rationale: Bayan as Enactment and Disclosure
Bayan (clarification) is partly insha' (enactment) and partly izhar (disclosure). After a wound, there remains room for enactment (the slave is alive), so the clarification is considered enactment. After death, no room remains, so it is pure disclosure.
This distinction explains the different outcomes in wound versus death cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (قال أحدكما حر فشجا فبين في أحدهما فارشهما للسيد) يعني إذا قال لعبديه أحدكما حر ثم شجا فبين في أحدهما العتق بعد الشج فأرشهما للمولى لأن العتق غير نازل في المعين فالشجة تصادف المعين فبقيا مملوكين في حق الشجة ولو قتلهما رجلا واحد في وقت واحد معاً تجب دية حر وقيمة عبد والفرق أن البيان إنشاء من وجه وإظهار من وجه على ما عرف وبعد الشجة بقي محلاً للبيان فاعتبر إنشاء في حق المحل وبعد الموت لم يبق محلاً للبيان فاعتبر إظهاراً محضاً، فإذا قتلهما رجل واحد معاً فأحدهما حر يجب عليه دية حر وقيمة عبد فيكون الكل نصفين بين المولى والورثة لعدم الأولوية، وإن اختلفت قيمتهما يجب نصف قيمة كل واحد منهما ودية حر فيقسم مثل الأول بخلاف ما إذا قتلهما على التعاقب حيث تجب عليه قيمة الأول لمولاه ودية الثاني للورثة، وبخلاف ما إذا قتل كل واحد منهما رجلاً معاً تجب قيمة المملوكين لأنا لم نتيقن بقتل كل واحد منهما حراً وكل منهما ينكر ذلك، ولأن البحر الرائق ج ٩/ م ١٢
He (the author) said: (He said: 'One of you is free' and he wounded, then he clarified (that the wounded one was the free one). In one of them, so the compensation for both is for the master.) Meaning, if he said to his two slaves: 'One of you is free', then he wounded (one of them), then he clarified (that the wounded one) was the freed one after the wound, then the compensation for both (wounds) is for the master, because the freeing was not attached to a specific person, so the wound occurred on a specific person, so both remained slaves with respect to the wound. If a single man kills both at the same time, then the blood money of a free person and the value of a slave are due. The difference is that clarification is partly an enactment and partly a disclosure, as is known. After the wound, there remains room for clarification, so it is considered an enactment with respect to the subject. After death, there is no room for clarification, so it is considered pure disclosure. So if one man kills both simultaneously, and one of them is (to be) free, then he owes the blood money of a free person and the value of a slave. The total is divided into halves between the master and the heirs, since there is no priority. If their values differ, then half the value of each and the blood money of a free person are owed, and it is divided similarly. This is different from if he killed them sequentially, where he owes the value of the first to his master and the blood money of the second to the heirs. And different from if each of them killed a man simultaneously, then the value of the two slaves is owed, because we are not certain that each of them killed a free person, and each denies that. And because... (end of page).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a man addresses his two slaves saying 'One of you is free' (without specifying which), then he wounds one of them, and later clarifies that the wounded one was the freed one. The ruling is that the compensation (arsh) for the wound is owed to the master (the original owner), because at the time of the wound, the freeing had not yet taken effect on a specific individual; the statement 'one of you is free' is ambiguous. The wound occurred while both were still slaves, so the master is the owner of the compensation. The clarification after the wound does not retroactively change the status of the slave at the moment of the wound. The passage distinguishes this from a case where both slaves are killed simultaneously by someone else: then the killer owes the full diyah for a free person (since one is free) and the value of a slave, divided between the master and the free person's heirs. If the killer kills them sequentially, he owes the value of the first slave to the master, and the diyah of the second (the free one) to the heirs. If each slave kills another person simultaneously, then the killer (each slave) owes the value of a slave (since their own status as 'free' is uncertain and they deny it). The reasoning hinges on whether clarification (bayan) is seen as enacting or disclosing, and whether the subject (the slave) still exists for clarification.

Key Terms
بيان (bayan): Clarification of an ambiguous statement; in this case, specifying which of the two slaves was freed after the wound.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a non-lethal injury to a slave, paid to the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a man says to his two slaves 'One of you is free', then wounds one, then clarifies that the wounded one was the freed one: to whom does the compensation (arsh) for the wound belong?
A) To the freed slave himself.
B) To the master (original owner).
C) Divided equally between the master and the freed slave.
D) To the heirs of the freed slave.

Key Takeaway
The compensation for an injury to a slave under an ambiguous declaration of freedom goes to the master because the slave's status at the time of injury is still slave; clarification does not retroactively change ownership.
